The SpaceX IPO, Fable 5, AI Capex Update & Market Check w/ Gavin Baker, Andrew Fox & Clark Tang

as a top-tier...

TL;DR

The upcoming SpaceX IPO at $135 per share (implied $1.77 trillion valuation) represents a generational, "must-own" asset, driven not only by the rapid scaling of its core launch and Starlink businesses, but by its sudden, highly profitable emergence as a top-tier terrestrial and orbital AI compute provider.

The SpaceX IPO: Core Levers and "Elon Web Services" (EWS)

  • Valuation & Projections: SpaceX’s upcoming IPO is priced at $135 per share, representing an implied $1.77 trillion valuation. Wall Street consensus projects the company will achieve $160 billion in revenue by 2028.
  • Rapid Reusability: The foundational metric for SpaceX's economics is rapid two-stage reusability of Starship.
      • Achieving this will drop payload launch costs from Falcon 9’s ~$1,500/kg to under $250/kg, driving launch cadences from hundreds to thousands of launches over the next three years (approximately 2–3 launches per day).
  • Starlink Hyper-Growth: Currently, global household broadband penetration for Starlink is under 1%. Revenue is projected to scale from $10 billion to $50 billion by 2028, fueled heavily by the rollout of Direct-to-Cell mobile connectivity.
  • Terrestrial AI Compute Powerhouse: Within 30 days, SpaceX has effectively become the world's 4th or 5th largest AI hyperscaler (dubbed "Elon Web Services" or EWS).
      • High-Margin Monetization: xAI’s Colossus 1 cluster boasts a calculated 55% IRR. EWS is monetizing compute at industry-leading rates: while Google generates high operating profit per gigawatt, SpaceX recently signed Anthropic at a $22B–$23B per-gigawatt rate, and Google at $50B per gigawatt.
      • Speed as a Moat: While typical enterprise supercomputers take three years to plan and a year to build, Elon Musk's team stood up a 100,000-GPU cluster in 122 days, getting it fully operational in just 19 days.

The Economic Case for Orbital (Space) Compute

  • Capital Expenditure Reductions: Terrestrial data centers currently cost roughly $60 billion per gigawatt to build ($35B for silicon, $25B for land, shell, power, and cooling).
      • Space data centers bypass land, cooling, and grid-power costs entirely. Even accounting for satellite failure rates, orbital compute CapEx is modeled at just $5 billion per gigawatt—a 5x reduction in non-silicon infrastructure costs.
  • Starship Payload Metrics: Starship V3's 100-metric-ton capacity is modeled to launch 5 megawatts of AI compute capacity per flight.
  • The "Call Option" Status: While terrestrial AI compute and Starlink are sufficient to justify the IPO valuation today, space-based data centers represent a massive, highly deflationary long-term call option as launch costs continue to decline.

Frontier AI Models, Cursor, and the Test-Time Compute Paradigm

  • The Cursor Acquisition Impact: xAI's quiet acquisition of the 700-to-800-person team behind Cursor (a leading coding agent on track to exit the year at nearly $10B in revenue) is a highly underappreciated asset.
      • Cursor's massive proprietary coding dataset is being injected directly into the pre-training phase of the 1.5-trillion-parameter Grok 4.3 model, positioning xAI to dominate the high-value coding agent market.
  • Frontier Dominance over Open Source: Contrary to predictions that cheap open-source tokens would commoditize the market, frontier closed-source models (such as Anthropic’s newly launched Fable 5/Mythos and OpenAI's models) are capturing over 90% of actual enterprise revenue.
  • Test-Time Compute: Static snapshot benchmarks are becoming obsolete. The frontier of AI is shifting toward "long-running" or "test-time" compute, where a model (like Fable 5) can run continuously for days or weeks on multi-agent orchestration tasks. Allowing a model to "think" longer yields massive leaps in practical intelligence.

The Trillion-Dollar AI CapEx Debate and Hardware Dynamics

  • CapEx Projections: Morgan Stanley recently raised its 2027 AI CapEx forecast to $1.1 trillion. Host Brad Gerstner projects the actual number—including non-hyperscalers like SpaceX and Coreweave—will exceed $1.5 trillion.
  • The Revenue Math: This massive CapEx is supported by rapidly scaling AI revenues. Leading labs are projected to achieve a combined $300 billion to $400 billion in inference revenue by 2027/2028 (with gross margins estimated at 60%–70%).
  • Nvidia's Moat: Despite attempts by tech giants to build custom ASICs (e.g., Google’s TPUs, Meta’s chips, or OpenAI's "Jalapeno" chip), Nvidia's market share remains unchallenged. Nvidia's GPUs deliver superior "tokens-per-watt," making alternative chips economically unviable in a power-constrained world.

Macro Market Check and Portfolio Strategy

  • Extreme Market Dispersion: Year-to-date, the Nasdaq and S&P 500 have been driven entirely by AI and semiconductor stocks. Meanwhile, the broader market has suffered, with the internet sector down 16% and software down 8%.
  • Managing Short-Term Volatility: AI token consumption typically plateaus seasonally during the summer months. Given this seasonality, rising inflation fears, and a massive run-up in semiconductor valuations, both Altimeter and Atreides have trimmed their exposure from "large" to "medium-small."
  • Long-Term Outlook: Despite short-term consolidation risks, the fundamental trajectory remains overwhelmingly positive. Over the last seven years, the Mag 7 added $1 trillion in revenue. Three companies alone—SpaceX, Anthropic, and OpenAI—are projected to add another trillion in revenue over the next four to five years.

Notable Quotes

"Imagine if Albert Einstein had been able... to think about fundamental physics 24 hours a day. He doesn't have to eat, he doesn't have to sleep... and never gets old, never dies, never has diminution of intelligence, and thought for one year. We might have already solved a lot of these intractable problems." — Gavin Baker

Today with Starship, they've shown that they can successfully re-land the booster. The second stage, we'll see what happens later this year. I think they're attempting to bring that back and then make it reusable by next year. But the thing that's important about two-stage reusability when it comes to the economics for orbital compute is the cost per kilogram comes down significantly. We're talking about going from $1,500 per kilogram on Falcon, somewhere in that range, to $250 per kilogram or something lower. The more you can reuse the rocket, the more that price comes down because you're just depreciating the cost of the launch, and eventually you asymptote to the cost of the fuel. Assume you can use a rocket forever—yes, which will take a very long time for us to really achieve—and at that point, we're talking about something well south of $250 per kilogram. So then you look at the specs of these AI satellites. Elon did a great—yeah, that pod was incredible that he laid out the other day, the specs on the satellites. It was really great because I think they are finally showing people how you could viably design one of these satellites, how heavy the satellite is, and how many you could fit into a Starship launch. When you back into the numbers, you get to something like 5 megawatts of capacity per Starship launch. There are 100 metric tons in one of those Starships, so you can back into the math of how much it will cost per gigawatt to launch these satellites and this compute into space. The math you get to, before you account for things like bad GPUs or bad satellites—which will all be things that happen—is that it is about $5 billion per gigawatt of CapEx to put these in space. For comparison terrestrially, when you talk about the switchgears, the generators, the transformers, the shell, and getting the power, that today is about $20 to $25 billion per gigawatt. So we're talking about a 5x reduction in cost on half of your bill of materials for the data center, which is a huge number. Google DeepMind's Logan Kilpatrick: Why the Model Eats the Harness

Sequoia Capital(213.0K)·51:09·8.0K views·2026-06-11
Logan KilpatrickLead, Google AI Studio and Gemini API, Google DeepMind
TL;DRAI agent harnesses have a roughly 12-month shelf life before foundational models natively absorb their scaffolding, prompting Google to anchor its ecosystem around "Anti-Gravity"—a single agent harness powering Search, Gemini, and Cloud, while…
"jagged" vertical...

TL;DR

AI agent harnesses have a roughly 12-month shelf life before foundational models natively absorb their scaffolding, prompting Google to anchor its ecosystem around "Anti-Gravity"—a single agent harness powering Search, Gemini, and Cloud, while "jagged" vertical superintelligences emerge in coding, science, and math.

The Transition to Agentic AI and the "Anti-Gravity" Harness

  • The Agentic Gemini Era: Google is transitioning its massive product suite from simple language model integration to agentic-native operations that proactively take actions on behalf of users.
  • The "Anti-Gravity" Connective Tissue: Born from the Windsurf team acquisition, the Anti-Gravity agent harness has replaced the raw Gemini model as the primary connective tissue across all Google products.
      • Cross-Product Deployment: Anti-Gravity powers agentic workflows across Google Search, the Gemini consumer app, Google Cloud, and Google AI Studio.
      • Specialization and Customization: While 80% of the underlying codebase is shared, the harness is optimized differently across surfaces—such as "vibe coding" in AI Studio versus always-on consumer behaviors in the Gemini app.
  • Outcome-Driven Metrics over "Eyeballs": Google is shifting its success metrics away from maximizing user attention and screen time toward maximizing user outcomes.
      • Positive-Sum AI Search: Contrary to early industry fears that generative answers would cannibalize Search traffic, Google has found that AI-integrated Search increases overall user query volume and ecosystem value.
  • The Crawl, Walk, Run Scale: Most of Google's consumer products are currently in the "crawl" phase. Given Google's responsibility in managing 13 products with over one billion users each, the company introduces agentic features incrementally to prevent sudden, disruptive changes to user habits.

Why the "Model Eats the Harness"

  • The Scaffolding Shelf Life: Logan Kilpatrick argues that the startup race to build external agent harnesses has a ~12-month window. Models will rapidly absorb these external layers and run them natively.
      • Upstreaming of Features: Capabilities that begin as external code orchestration (e.g., tool calling, secure code execution, containerized model runs) are consistently absorbed directly into foundational model weights and native model systems over time.
  • Shifting Value Capture for Startups: Because the foundational model will inevitably "eat" the infrastructure layer, application developers cannot rely on custom harnesses for long-term defensibility.
      • Domain Expertise as the New Edge: Defensibility will reside in deep vertical expertise, proprietary workflows, user trust, and focus.
      • Focus as a Startup Superpower: Unlike giant companies that must serve thousands of diverse user needs simultaneously, startups can dominate specific niches by moving faster and embracing a higher risk tolerance.
  • Industry Standard Benchmarking: To measure how effectively different frontier models adapt to various developer structures, Kilpatrick calls for the creation of an industry-standard "Harness Bench" evaluation.

Coding and the Rise of "Jagged" Superintelligence

  • Narrow Superintelligence in Coding: Autonomous coding already exhibits properties of narrow superintelligence, drastically accelerating software creation and resetting human developer ambition.
      • Rapid Internal Prototyping: Google teams are utilizing Anti-Gravity to build and ship complex software, such as Google's native macOS app, faster than ever before.
      • Massive User Adoption: Over 350,000 Android apps have been built inside Google AI Studio in a single week, enabling non-traditional developers to build highly localized, personal software.
  • Post-Training Dominance: Highlighting recent engineering efficiencies, Gemini 1.5 Flash achieved its massive coding and reasoning gains over previous Pro models entirely through post-training optimization rather than new pre-training runs.
  • "Jagged" Vertical Superintelligence: Rather than achieving general AGI uniformly, the industry will first see "jagged" superintelligence across specific, highly verifiable verticals, including:
      • Mathematics and formal proofs.
      • Quantitative finance and crypto-related personal productivity tools.
      • Scientific research and therapeutic discovery.
  • Vibe Coding Video Games: Kilpatrick stands by his prediction that average users will be able to "vibe code" playable video games by the end of 2025.
      • Current Bottlenecks: While the core coding capabilities exist, game development remains bottlenecked by the orchestration of non-code elements, such as native sprite generation, Three.js scaffolding, and asset rendering.

World Models and the Unified "Omni" Architecture

  • The Omni Architecture: Google’s Omni represents a shift from training eight distinct, routed models (for text, audio, music, images, and video) to a single, natively multimodal model.
  • Redefining World Models: Omni acts as a true world model by displaying a physical understanding of reality, allowing for highly nuanced, context-aware video editing in real-time.
      • Subtle Contextual Awareness: In a live demonstration, Omni inserted a digital dog onto a physical stage; the model adjusted the video so that human speakers naturally glanced down, chuckled, and adjusted their posture to acknowledge the digital animal.
  • Generative Media and Authenticity: Kilpatrick views generative media as an accelerant for human creativity, not a replacement for human presence.
      • Content Preservation: Rather than generating synthetic AI avatars, tools like Omni allow creators to preserve their authentic words, voice, and appearance while instantly swapping and customizing background sets, environments, and digital objects.

Google DeepMind's Scientific Culture and Strategy

  • Scientific DNA: Google DeepMind (GDM) approaches artificial intelligence through a deeply academic and scientific lens. Kilpatrick compares the cultures of the top AI labs to the DNA of their founders:
      • Google DeepMind: Scientific, mission-driven, and focused on solving fundamental real-world challenges (led by Nobel laureate Demis Hassabis).
      • OpenAI: Commercial, strategic, and business-first (led by Sam Altman).
      • Anthropic: Highly safety-focused and intellectually esoteric (led by Dario Amodei).
  • The Engine Room of Google: Officially serving as the "engine room of Google," GDM balances long-horizon research with the immediate, applied engineering challenge of scaling Gemini models to a massive global footprint.

Notable Quotes

"Success for Google probably doesn't look like maximizing eyeball time in front of our products. It's like maximizing outcomes for customers to do the thing that they want to do so that they can go and live their life." — Logan Kilpatrick
"The scaffolding is often times a couple of steps ahead of what is baked directly into the model. And then what ends up happening is the model eats that scaffolding and it becomes part of the native model system... The alpha will be somewhere else now." — Logan Kilpatrick
"We're all fighting over who can make the world better more than the other person, which when you frame it like that, seems really goofy to me. It is very much not zero-sum." — Logan Kilpatrick

Success for Google probably doesn't look like, you know, maximizing eyeball time in front of our products. It's like maximizing outcomes for customers to do the thing that they want to do so that they can go and live their life and do what they want. And so I feel like you'll probably see us go down the route of maximizing outcomes for customers and not maximizing eyeballs. Yeah. I have this term stuck in my head: agent-led growth. I'm like, "I don't care which database, you tell me." If you were to, you know, grade the scale of agenticness in terms of crawl, walk, run, where are we in terms of how agentic the Google suite of products is? Yeah, that's a great question. And so, for example, having a 24/7 always-on agent, like, literally going and potentially doing a bunch of actions on your behalf, is definitely, like, one of the frontier use cases. Yeah. Do you think that Google ends up with one, two, three product surfaces for using AI, or thousands? It's tough. I think a lot of this is actually baked in just, like, how humans consume products. I think maybe somebody will spin together, like, the truly magic experience that doesn't make that true. This is my hot take for why slide decks have existed for so long—just, like, you know, the piece of information you want is exactly in the same place. Excitingly, it's 350,000 apps that probably no one was going to build before. A lot of these are personal, too. The web is definitely powerful, but there are certain things that the operating systems have that you just can't unlock—like lots of native richness that actually makes experiences feel so much richer. I think about this for text messaging, actually. The text messaging experience in all the main operating systems feels way richer to me than any AI chat app that I've ever used. If I could just talk to AI in whatever text messaging app I use, I would be way happier than having to go to some other app, because we are also just conditioned on the operating systems. Yeah, makes sense. Okay. I want to ask about "the model eats the harness," or "the model eats the scaffolding." What are your thoughts? Yeah, I think it's true. And part of this is that what we have historically thought of as "the model" is not the model anymore. Two years ago when LLMs were popular, the model was actually just a set of weights. It was a set of weights, and it was really about how to, as simply as possible, send tokens in and get tokens out. We've just progressively, step by step, gone beyond that. We still call it the model—you still call it Gemini 1.5, GPT-whatever, or Claude-whatever—but it's actually not just the weights anymore. It's an entire, expanding, sprawling system built around the weights that enables a lot of these next-generation experiences, from agentic tool calling to hosted tools, Search, code execution, etc. The models are now being spun up in containers and sort of have an agent harness and all that stuff. So the scaffolding is often a couple of steps ahead of what is baked directly into the model, and then what ends up happening is the model eats that scaffolding and it becomes part of the native model system. There's still value in having external scaffolding in certain cases—Search maybe is an example of this. Lots of folks use different search providers, and there are different use cases that you want, so while the model can natively use Search, you also want something else. Code execution is another example of that. But it does feel like maybe the agent harness is the quintessential example of this right now, where everyone is like, "We've got to go build a harness, and the harness is where the alpha is." I think that perhaps won't be true, at least in the way that we think of the harness today, in 12 months. The models will have sort of just digested a bunch of that. It'll be upstreamed into the model, and the alpha will be somewhere else now. It won't be in trying to spin your own harness because the model just does it natively. But I thought that part of the reason why people are building their own harnesses is because if you use a harness from any given model provider, you're locked in, right? So a lot of the application companies want flexibility, which is why they're building their own harnesses. Yeah, and I think that's part of the scaffolding story. That starts out perhaps true, but then as the model capability improves, it becomes less true over time, actually. You don't have a generalized model if it can't use another harness. It is important—and I mentioned this in another conversation with someone a few weeks ago—that we need something like "Harness Bench," which actually measures how good all these different models are at adapting to different harnesses. I feel like that's a reasonable thing we should measure as an ecosystem, and I'd be curious to see what models are actually best. But over time, you expect they'd be able to use every harness, unless you're completely out of distribution, in which case you're still going to be completely out of distribution even if you're using your own harness. So I'm not sure it matters much. What about the application layer? How do you think about where independent companies can have a hope of surviving when the model eats the harness and eats the stuff around it? It feels like—yeah, it's an interesting story where both of these things feel true. On one hand, everywhere I look, I'm like, "There has never been more opportunity to go and build something." At the same time, obviously, the models are doing more than they've ever done before. I think there's that thread of capability overhang, which I think has a huge amount of alpha in it. There's also the thread where the model companies are going after these very general problems, and there's just so much value in these verticalized domains. If you have expertise in that domain, you sort of know the customers, you know the ecosystem, and you can really run laps around even the best model labs. Focus is the superpower of startups. If you can focus, you can do anything. And if you look at all of the companies that are big or doing lots of stuff, there's just not a lot of focus. For some reasons, that's rightfully so—maybe I'm overly justifying Google's strategy—but we just have a lot of products, a lot of users, and a lot of different things going on, so we actually can't focus on one domain. We have an obligation to do a bunch of things as a big company. That's not true for startups. I think 24 months ago, we were all asking ourselves, "Ooh, wow, it seems like the opportunity space is shifting, and maybe it's possible one of the outcomes is there is less opportunity for startups in the future." That feels so far away from what has ended up playing out, which is really positive. If anything, it feels like there is even more opportunity than there was. Coding has helped you close the gap on larger companies that have established codebases and all this other stuff, because you can just run way faster and write software quicker. The agentic primitive is a new category you can build products around. In a lot of cases, to the conversation about the risks involved with building—there's risk involved, and the risk appetite of different companies is different. If you're willing to take more risk in some domains, you can win a user cohort who's interested in also taking risk. So there is so much opportunity. Awesome. Yossi Matias on the golden age of research

Google for Developers(2.6M)·71:35·633 views·2026-06-11
Yossi MatiasVP & Head of Google Research, Google Logan KilpatrickHost, Google for Developers
TL;DRGoogle Research is driving a new "golden age of research" by accelerating the "magic cycle" of innovation—an iterative loop that rapidly translates breakthrough scientific discoveries in climate, medicine, and quantum computing into scalable, real-world tools that amplify human capability.

TL;DR

Google Research is driving a new "golden age of research" by accelerating the "magic cycle" of innovation—an iterative loop that rapidly translates breakthrough scientific discoveries in climate, medicine, and quantum computing into scalable, real-world tools that amplify human capability.

The "Magic Cycle" of Climate and Planetary Intelligence

  • The Magic Cycle of Research: Yossi Matias defines this as the self-reinforcing loop where asking a fundamental question leads to breakthrough research, publication, and real-world application, which in turn prompts the next scientific question. This cycle is now accelerating faster than ever due to AI acting as its own accelerator.
  • Google Earth AI: Google Research is consolidating its various geospatial, weather, and remote sensing models into a single agentic platform.
      • Global Flood Forecasting: A system once deemed "impossible" by experts now operates in 150 countries, protecting 2 billion people by predicting river floods up to 7 days in advance. In Nigeria, governments use the API to distribute evacuation funds to villagers before floods hit.
      • Urban Flash Floods: To address the lack of training data for city flash floods, researchers used Gemini to scan public reports, synthesizing a high-quality dataset of 2.6 million flash flood events (a technique called "groundsource") to train operational prediction models.
      • NeuralGCM: Google's collaborative weather model with the University of Chicago has helped provide early weather warnings to 38 million farmers in India.

Medicine and Healthcare AI Milestones

  • Med-Gemma Open Platform: Google’s open-weight medical model has surpassed 5 million downloads.
      • Offline Medical Access: In Kampala, Uganda, healthcare workers utilize Med-Gemma offline on mobile devices to diagnose life-threatening conditions like eclampsia in villages lacking internet connectivity, successfully reducing maternal mortality.
  • Clinical Screening Advancements:
      • Diabetic Retinopathy: Ten years after Google’s landmark JAMA paper proved AI could screen for diabetic retinopathy at an expert level, the technology is fully operational in Thailand and India, providing retinal scans and results to clinic patients in just 2 minutes.
      • Mammography Screening: A recent Google study published in *Nature* with the UK NHS demonstrated that using AI as a "second reader" in mammograms identified 25% of missed cancer cases while giving 40% of their time back to radiologists.
      • Clinical Communication: Google is piloting AMIE (Articulate Medical Intelligence Explorer) with Included Health to test conversational, diagnostic AI support in active clinics.

"Gemini for Science" and Automated Discovery

  • Gemini for Science Launch: Google has consolidated its frontier scientific AI tools into a unified suite designed to act as a "polymath in your pocket." The suite includes:
      • AI Co-Scientist: A multi-agent virtual lab helper that performs vast cross-disciplinary literature searches and generates novel hypotheses. In a pilot with Imperial College, Co-Scientist generated a bacterium hypothesis in just two days that had previously taken human researchers a decade to formulate.
      • Empirical Research Assistant (ERA): An automated system that searches parameter spaces and tunes computational models for scorable problems, replacing weeks of manual coding. Its release in *Nature* was accompanied by eight distinct peer-reviewed papers in fields ranging from cosmology to economics.
      • AlphaFold & NotebookLM: Integrated into the suite to provide protein structure predictions and tailored literature insights.

Algorithmic Efficiency and Generative UI

  • Speculative Decoding: Google Research developed this algorithmic technique to accelerate LLM inference.
      • Performance Gains: The algorithm doubles or triples inference speed (latency and throughput) on Gemini 1.5 without any loss in output quality.
      • Industry Standard: The technique has effectively doubled the global capacity of existing chips, allowing the industry to run models as if they had twice the hardware resources.
  • Generative UI (Gen-UI): Developed in collaboration with the Google Search team, Gen-UI uses AI to dynamically determine the most effective visual presentation format for search and chat content in real time based on user context.

AI Education and Quantum Computing Milestones

  • Reimagining Education: Google is focusing on adapting AI to enhance and personalize learning through specialized initiatives:
      • LearnLM: A model fine-tuned for educational pedagogy, enabling daily assessments for students in Ghana and saving teachers up to five hours of manual grading per week.
      • Learn Your Way: An experimental program designed to turn static textbooks into personalized, multi-modal experiences (e.g., teaching physics concepts like gravity using soccer examples for a 10-year-old vs. tennis for a 16-year-old).
      • Future Skills: Matias emphasizes that basic computer science remains essential because "it is not learning a language, it's learning how to think," while soft skills like collaboration will become primary differentiators.
  • Quantum Computing Breakthroughs:
      • Nobel Recognition: Google's Quantum AI Lab (founded in 2012) celebrated the physics Nobel Prize awarded to long-time lab scientists and collaborators Michel Devoret, John Martinis, and John Clarke for foundational work dating back to the 1980s.
      • The Willow Chip: Google's latest quantum hardware achieved major milestones in quantum error correction and demonstrated "verifiable quantum advantage," solving a benchmark math problem 13,000 times faster than the world’s most powerful classical supercomputers.

Notable Quotes

"Computer science is not learning a language; it's learning how to think. In a way, it's going to be more important than ever for the same reason that math is important even when calculators became a commodity." — Yossi Matias
"This notion of empowerment to healthcare workers, to teachers, to business people, to scientists, is to me one of the most exciting aspects of AI. I think about it as AI as an amplifier of human ingenuity." — Yossi Matias
"Technology innovation and scientific progress is not linear. That's actually a common mistake... Our job at Google Research is to look into the horizons and around the corner. It's not to extrapolate." — Yossi Matias

My personal experience, actually, watching a nearby fire 15 years ago got me into understanding how important it is to get timely information. Which, later, actually led me to taking on and leading what we call crisis resilience, launching in Search SOS alerts, which now has had billions of views on natural disasters and COVID and other things. We launched it, actually, about 10 years ago, and at the time, I discovered that this is all great, we're very helpful—actually, got some notes about various incidents that people say, "Hey, thanks for doing it, this saved our lives." But I discovered, also, that the most devastating natural disasters in terms of people impact, which are floods, were not helpful because, in order to be helpful, you need to actually warn people ahead of the flood. And, in fact, I remember going to India, to Bihar, getting to a village that was hit by a flood just a few days before overnight, and a few people perished. And there's always this personal story because there were three young men who actually saved quite a few people overnight, swimming with them to, to safety. Seeing the villagers, seeing the aftermath—you know, the entire village, more or less, covered with water—you realize, you know, what we're missing is a warning. And then I had conversations with many experts—hydrology, flood experts—and everybody was telling me, "Hey, this is too difficult to solve. Too many variables, you cannot really provide a trusted warning that people can actually take action." We decided it's important to try anyway, and that, that's what we do. And, by the way, I have yet to see a problem that is too difficult to solve. It's a question of how much effort you need to put into that. Can we use machine learning and cloud, build some models, simulations? And I asked, actually, an engineer to show me some progress, and once he showed me some progress, I said, "What do they need to run a pilot?" And within a couple of months, he actually ran a small—in India terms—pilot, covering about 1 million people, and we saw that we can actually do some, some modeling, prediction. Started to see enough signs that we were brave enough to publish a paper in a NeurIPS workshop with a hypothesis that we would be able to scale it with machine learning and other techniques. And we started iterating, expanded the pilot times 10, covered all of India at some point, and then pushed the science enough to actually publish a paper in Nature about a global hydrologic model which could benefit from all the data that we see globally, even benefit areas that do not have data that you can train models on. That enabled us to scale, and today we have flood prediction in 150 countries covering 2 billion people. Up to 7 days in advance. This is life-saving. Yeah. And this is for a problem that was considered impossible just a few years ago. But, even then, you still hit another wall because this was for river floods where, you know, flood is going ahead, river is going over the board, and this was not helpful for flash floods, which are also devastating, as we've seen in the last couple of years. And there were no good datasets on which you can build machine learning models. In fact, the innovation here came just a few recently. We published a technique we call Groundsource. And the idea was taking Gemini, using public data—because flash floods, at least in cities, urban flash floods, are important in news. So, we said: let's take Gemini, apply it to all the public data that we have, and try to distill from that flash flood events. And we did it. We actually constructed a high-quality dataset of 2.6 million flash flood events that we could feed into a machine learning model and build a prediction model. And now it's actually in operation. So, we now have flash flood modeling—at least an important step towards that—based on Groundsource, on, again, iterating on this magic cycle: pilots and research papers, and more research, and building a system, and partnering, by the way, with others. We're partnering with the WMO, meteorologist teams in those countries, and making it available. And now it's part of Earth AI. Just a few months ago, we had the government of Nigeria actually using the API to actually see where flood is going to hit and send money to villagers ahead of the flood so they can evacuate. And similarly, we have a partnership with GiveDirectly, again, who send funds, resources to villagers so they can actually evacuate ahead of time and keep themselves safe, as well as take actions to protect their properties. These are examples of something that, just a decade ago, was kind of out of reach. With our kind of approach to technology, of research, of building systems, of partnership, we made it something that actually can now be beneficial for society. This is beautiful. There's many more examples to talk through which I'm excited about. But actually, maybe it's worth just like opining for a, for a moment. Yeah, obviously AI is transformative. It's touching all aspects of society. For actually really quick, for folks—and I should have said this before—for folks who don't sort of understand the prominence of Nature, like give just like the really quick context of like why, why Nature in publications is important. First, it's important to know that when we're talking about scientific research, or research in general, one thing that is more important than ever is the scientific method. In fact, arguably one of the biggest successes of humanity is creating the scientific method, where we can actually build innovation and knowledge and then have it validated by the community, the scientific community, in a way that we can actually trust to build on that, to build the next layer. So, science and research is this pretty amazing, if you will, cathedral of knowledge, built layer by layer by layer. Right? I mean, I think it was Newton who said, "I'm standing on the shoulders of giants." That's actually what science is about; it's about building this, this cathedral of knowledge, these layers of knowledge. In order to build those, you need to actually have validation. That's why peer-reviewed publications are significant, so you can actually trust that what has been discovered doesn't only look good, but it was actually validated to the extent possible by the experts in the field. When we talk about the more, the more prestigious, if you will, publications such as Nature, then obviously the importance is that it tests the merit of the scientific results in a very rigorous way. Both in terms of its contribution to science, in terms of the validation of the claims made there, to make sure that experimentations were done in the right way, etc., etc. So, that's why these scientific milestones make a difference, especially in a time that you have so many publications out there. Very much related, when you think about the proliferation of publications—and, by the way, not only peer-reviews, I think that publications in arXiv is one of the amazing phenomena that we see in recent years. Another acceleration of this, both the research cycle, if you will, but also the magic cycle of research between identifying, learning, and applying it. And here entered one of the capabilities of a system that we called AI Co-Scientist. Which is asking ourselves: can we use AI to actually help us with, first, literature search, sifting through all the literature out there for any research question that I'm posing? So, I want to have some drug repurposing for a particular condition, and I want to scan, I want to read the entire literature that may be relevant. By the way, importantly, not all the relevant literature is obviously relevant; sometimes you need to actually read through to look for those hints. Also, importantly, sometimes literature that is relevant for your research is from, not necessarily, only your domain. And, in fact, quite often, the magic happens when you bring together insights from multiple domains, which is quite difficult to do in real life because, especially among scientists, people are encouraged to actually be experts in one area—they need to, in order to be at the front line. And then, when you want to connect disciplines, it's actually quite difficult, and there aren't too many polymaths out there, you know, in the state of the art of more than one discipline. With AI Co-Scientist, which is a multi-agent system that is meant for a given research question posed by a scientist, to do literature search practically as vast as needed, but then, importantly, also given the question, the research question, start creating hypotheses, generating hypotheses that are relevant to that question. And then, once you can start doing these hypotheses, obviously you may have a plethora of hypotheses, so an important question is: how do you actually filter? How do you actually decide which ones actually are worth exploring? And, also, how do we rank them? Because, you know, it's like search results—it's not only about how many pages you, you find, but what are the top 10 for consideration for the scientist. So, ranking is important, validation, of course, is important. Here's a hypothesis: to what extent can I see that it's actually consistent with literature that I find? Co-Scientist is doing that; it's actually doing the literature search, it's doing the hypothesis generation. So, think about it as a virtual lab that is actually doing all this work that, typically, a very senior researcher would do for more junior researchers, helping them—grad students, postdocs, and others. And what we found out is that this can actually be a huge acceleration for science. So, in an early partnership that we had with Imperial College, they were studying certain bacteria for, you know, for years. Actually, it took them almost a decade to come up with a certain hypothesis; they didn't publish it yet. And then, with AI Co-Scientist, they got the same hypothesis in just a couple of days. And, importantly, AI Co-Scientist also presented additional hypotheses they were not considering. And I was speaking with Tiago, the professor in Imperial College who was actually interacting with the system, asked him: "So, how was it to work with AI Co-Scientist?" Why can't you have assessment that is more personalized, that is real-time, as if you have a tutor sitting next to you and actually tapping you on your shoulder every time you need to do some correction? That's actually the real opportunity. Another important comment here is that the role of the teacher is more important than ever. You mentioned before... we won the Nobel, a bunch of the researchers won the Nobel Prize for quantum. That kind of shined a light on a lot of the breakthroughs that have been happening. But folks who have heard bits and pieces maybe think quantum is science fiction. What is Google actually doing today in this space? So, I think quantum is an interesting story. Obviously, some of the early pioneering thinking came from Richard Feynman, making the case of why quantum could be a big deal one day. The big breakthrough was Peter Shor actually showing a real, practical algorithm that today some people would say is perhaps too practical, actually factoring numbers. And by the way, Peter's office was just across from mine in Bell Labs, which was nice. He showed that with a quantum computer, you could actually solve a problem that is assumed to be hard for classical computers. Actually, it's so much assumed to be hard that it's the basis for many of the cryptosystems that are used in the industry. And he showed that if one day we are going to have a quantum computer, we're actually going to be able to solve it. Now, by now we have actually a dozen or so—perhaps 70 or so—kind of known applications of where quantum could actually make a significant acceleration over classical computers. But importantly, there are some additional expectations that if we have quantum computers, we're going to be able to actually have a better understanding of materials. We can actually simulate materials. We can have quantum sensing. And for simulating materials as an example, it's just like... some of those simulations are just so computationally intensive that— Not only that, you need to have the intermolecular... you need to have the quantum phenomena. So this was actually part of the statement by Feynman, that, hey, in order to actually simulate a quantum world, you need a quantum computer. This was kind of the thesis. But importantly, my projection is that when you think about what is known—that a quantum computer is going to have an advantage over the classical world—suppose what would happen if you take thousands of bright people working on actually developing more applications. Think about all the huge numbers of amazing talent working today on AI and machine learning, and you see a proliferation of progress. Just think about a fraction of that actually working on quantum. I'm sure we're going to see many more opportunities to actually unlock, because it's a new computing paradigm. Now the real question is, then: can we build a quantum computer, and what is the technology to do that? Some of the breakthroughs that are enabling that go back to the 80s. We were really thrilled with the Nobel Prize in Physics going to Michel Devoret in our Quantum AI Lab, and also John Martinis, who used to be part of that lab, and John Clarke, the collaborator from Berkeley—the three of them were recognized with the Nobel Prize for their work in the 80s. We built our Quantum AI Lab in 2012. Actually, really quick: why—why the gap? I'm not super... I saw the announcement and stuff, but why the gap between the work in the 80s to being recognized in, I think it was 2024 or something? Was it just that it took that long for it to be shown actually that the work was going to prove out or had been proven out, and then folks just... you know, quantum's becoming more real, so there's more scientific interest in the ecosystem? Or why such a big gap in that area? Well, I cannot speak for the considerations of the Nobel committee, but the big question over the years was: will we be able to build a quantum computer that actually can do the work? There are many questions, including one of the big technological hurdles: in order to have a scalable quantum computer, you need to solve the problem of errors. And you cannot just apply error correction like you do with classical computers, so this notion of quantum error correction is increasingly important. Over the years, we did have significant milestones. Our own milestone just last year was actually the Willow chip, which had a couple of additional milestones with it. One was an error correction that was tremendously better than previously known, which actually showed promise that we can actually keep scaling quantum computing with error correction. And quantum error correction, just to double-click on that, is really when, because the basic operations are dealing with qubits, in this physical world not all operations actually work, and then you need to somehow compensate for that. So how do you actually deal with faulty qubits? Error correction is helping to do that, much in the same way that things happen also in a regular computer where you use error correction on a classical computer in order to compensate for that. We had a few milestones. The most recent one is called verifiable quantum advantage, namely a problem where we could actually apply quantum computing, and we could verify and compare it to the best classical computer that could solve it. We showed that for a particular problem, we could actually do that 13,000 times faster. This is kind of a verification on a problem that is different from a mathematical Shor's algorithm, showing that, hey, there are certain computations that with a quantum computer we can actually do much, much faster than classical. Now, it's still in the early stages, and we still need to keep building the systems. We have very clear roadmaps of how to reach those, and again, it's a combination of an amazing hardware team and applications on quantum. We keep actually hitting these milestones, and the promise there is quite significant, obviously also accelerating AI in some capacity. It's not going to replace classical computers, but it's going to complement them, and for certain problems, it's going to unlock opportunities never seen before—such as generated data, for example, for which we can possibly build AI models that can help us better understand materials, biology information, fusion, and so forth. Anyway, these are some of the premises, and it's really exciting to see how we're making progress on that. This is actually based on work done in the 80s, but we keep iterating on it, so the magic cycle of research here takes longer but is quite significant. Yeah, there's so much stuff happening. Keeping track of this... I don't know if we can put a metric on the pace of the magic cycle and sort of watch as things happen, like the speed up. This would be a fun benchmark. I don't know how to do this, but this might be a push for you—you should make that. I feel like it'd be super interesting to see. All the work that I have been talking about was made possible by amazing teams that are working together across great collaborations, obviously within Google Research, but also with colleagues in Google DeepMind and across Google. A lot of that is also with the academic community. This notion of actually having smart people who are driven by making real impact, working in collaboration, and then applying it to some of the best systems in the world is powerful. We are applying it to products that are actually serving billions of people, helping in so many different ways. Then we are iterating and thinking about what's next, what's around the corner, and what's on the horizon—how to take those bets so we can actually drive things that perhaps may seem impossible and make them possible. That's actually a pretty amazing opportunity. That's why it's really motivating and more exciting than ever to do research. It's really a golden age of research. Yeah, it's very exciting. Well, Yossi, thank you again for sitting down and talking about sort of the golden age of research. Former CFTC Chair Gary Gensler on regulating sports betting: Let the states do it

CNBC Television(3.4M)·10:15·3.7K views·2026-06-11
Gary GenslerFormer CFTC Chairman & Former SEC Chairman
TL;DRFormer CFTC and SEC Chairman Gary Gensler is filing an amicus brief in the Sixth Circuit arguing that Congress never intended for the Commodity Futures Trading Commission (CFTC) to regulate sports betting under the 2010 Dodd-Frank Act.
Gensler contends that sports betting...

TL;DR

Former CFTC and SEC Chairman Gary Gensler is filing an amicus brief in the Sixth Circuit arguing that Congress never intended for the Commodity Futures Trading Commission (CFTC) to regulate sports betting under the 2010 Dodd-Frank Act. Gensler contends that sports betting and prediction markets should remain under state jurisdiction, as the CFTC lacks the resources, staff, and legislative mandate to police them.

Legislative Intent of Dodd-Frank and the Amicus Brief

  • Sixth Circuit Filing: Gensler is filing an amicus brief to explain the original context of the 2010 Dodd-Frank Act.
      • He notes that Dodd-Frank was passed during a financial crisis to address credit default swaps and interest rate swaps, not to nationalize sports betting regulation.
  • Lack of Congressional Mandate: During his 54 congressional testimonies as CFTC Chairman, no lawmakers from either party suggested the agency should regulate sports betting.
      • Harry Reid's Position: Gensler asserts that former Senate Majority Leader Harry Reid (D-NV) would have "thrown [him] out of his office" if there had been any attempt to strip states of their betting regulatory powers.

The Case for State-Level Regulation

  • Bipartisan State Coalition: A group of 39 states—representing red, blue, and purple states—recently filed a joint amicus brief in the same Sixth Circuit case to protect state-level regulation.
  • Resource Advantage: States possess the revenue, law enforcement, and regulatory infrastructure to manage sports betting, whereas the CFTC has shrunk and lacks the budget.
  • Local Policy Autonomy: States are better positioned to tailor laws to their local communities.
      • Currently, 11 states prohibit sports betting entirely.
      • States can set customized age limits (e.g., 21 instead of 18) and address local public health concerns like gambling addiction and the "dopamine rush" associated with youth betting.

The CFTC’s Regulatory Limits and Recent Actions

  • Historical Scope: The CFTC was established in the 1970s to oversee agricultural, energy, and financial derivatives, not millions of daily retail sports wagers.
  • Staffing Constraints: The CFTC is a small agency of only about 400 employees and has recently undergone further downsizing.
  • Reversing Past Precedents: The CFTC is currently proposing a 200+ page rule change that Gensler argues attempts to reverse a unanimous 2011 CFTC ruling.
      • The 2011 rule prohibited contracts on assassination, war, terrorism, gaming, and unlawful acts.
      • Preemptive Lawsuits: The CFTC has taken the unusual step of preemptively filing lawsuits against various states (such as Arizona and New York) to assert federal jurisdiction over prediction markets.

Prediction Markets and Insider Trading Vulnerabilities

  • High Sports Betting Volume: Between 70% and 80% of activity on major prediction platforms like Polymarket and Kalshi is sports betting, putting them in direct competition with state-regulated entities like FanDuel and DraftKings.
  • Insider Trading Risks: Ensuring market integrity is exceptionally difficult on prediction platforms compared to traditional stock markets.
      • Unlike corporate equities with established fiduciary duties, prediction markets are highly vulnerable to insiders (e.g., corporate employees or political consultants) trading on non-public, proprietary information.
  • Government Leaks: Gensler warns that government insiders could leak information from the White House Situation Room or Capitol Hill to trade on geopolitical or legislative contracts.
      • "Eddie Murphy Provision": While Dodd-Frank successfully banned trading on inside government information (inspired by the movie *Trading Places*), Gensler urges Congress to go further and completely ban government employees from trading on prediction markets.

Notable Quotes

"At the core of this issue is, did Congress in 2010 say no, none of the states can regulate this, it's going to this little small agency that I once was proud to run? And the answer is categorically no." — Gary Gensler
"It's an agency that was set up in the 1970s around corn, wheat, agricultural derivatives, and then energy and interest rate derivatives... to think that you're going to regulate millions of transactions of whether somebody's going to hit a three-point play [is absurd]." — Gary Gensler
"I also think Congress would be well advised to say listen, nobody in the government should trade on these things. You're a congressional staffer, you're executive branch or White House staffer... Let's get it over the line." — Gary Gensler

No. Look, Joe, you've raised a really good point. Prediction markets were raised many years ago by the defense research bureau called DARPA inside the Defense Department. Customer Ignite Talk: Antonio Bravo Acin (Global Head of AI Transformation, BBVA) & OpenAI

OpenAI(2.0M)·18:42·434 views·2026-06-11
Antonio Bravo AcinGlobal Head of AI Transformation, BBVA
TL;DRBBVA has integrated generative AI across its global operations of 120,000 employees through "The Eight" framework—comprising six specialized functional AI robots and two technical pillars.
By combining a business-sponsored, top-down strategy with active bottom-up...

TL;DR

BBVA has integrated generative AI across its global operations of 120,000 employees through "The Eight" framework—comprising six specialized functional AI robots and two technical pillars. By combining a business-sponsored, top-down strategy with active bottom-up experimentation, the bank is actively scaling AI from isolated pilot projects into an industrialized, agent-driven operating layer.

"The Eight" Framework: Six Robots and Two Pillars

BBVA's AI strategy is anchored by six specialized "robots" representing high-impact business areas, supported by two foundational pillars:

  • Robot 1 (Retail Business): Creating conversational, multimodal digital channels on smartphones to reinvent how retail customers interact with banking services.
  • Robot 2 (Advisory & Commercial Banking): Empowering bankers in corporate, private, and enterprise banking to increase customer face-time.
      • The goal is to shift customer advisory time from the historical average of 20%–25% up to 30%–35%+.
  • Robot 3 (Risk Management): Providing risk analysts with advanced AI tools to accelerate credit underwriting and improve customer onboarding experiences.
  • Robot 4 (Process & Back-Office Operations): Automating document extraction and classification for heavily administrative processes, such as mortgages, insurance, and consumer finance paperwork.
  • Robot 5 (Software Development): Integrating OpenAI’s Codex across developer teams to clear project backlogs and optimize software engineering capacity.
  • Robot 6 (Connected Robot / General Adoption): Distributing ChatGPT Enterprise licenses to all 120,000 global employees to build general-purpose agents for scheduling, email, and HR tools.
  • Two Core Pillars: Becoming a "data-rich" company to make data consumable by autonomous agents, and building a centralized orchestration infrastructure to manage and continuously improve the resulting network of agents.

Operating Model & Adoption Mechanics

To transition from theoretical AI strategy to bank-wide utility, BBVA relies on active change management and structural peer pressure:

  • Business-Led Sponsorship: The AI agenda is not run in isolation by the technology department. Each "robot" is co-led and funded directly by business unit heads (e.g., the Head of Corporate and Investment Banking co-sponsors Robot 2).
  • Executive Adoption Dashboards: The bank sends monthly adoption metrics directly to the executive committee, including the Chairman and CEO.
      • These dashboards track personal tool usage, benchmarking leaders against their peers to foster healthy executive competition and drive top-down adoption.
  • Bottom-Up Innovation ("Planting Grass"): By distributing licenses broadly, BBVA allows employees to find organic use cases before formalizing them into enterprise paths.
      • This has resulted in more than 100 employee-built GPTs that are actively used by thousands of employees, driving 70% to 80% time savings on specific workflows.
  • Aha Moments and Stage-Gated Funding: Instead of long-term speculative budgets, BBVA funds AI projects iteratively based on tangible "aha moments" delivered every two to three months.

Ecosystem Partnerships & Industrializing the Future

As the technology rapidly shifts, BBVA is focusing on agility over fixed legacy architectures:

  • Mitigating Obsolescence via OpenAI: Partnering closely with OpenAI has allowed BBVA to quickly abandon obsolete technical paths. The bank has made several "180-degree flips" in its technical direction based on direct guidance from OpenAI, avoiding stranded assets.
  • Industrializing Agentic Capabilities: BBVA is transitioning from building bespoke individual agents to building a standardized, industrial-scale playbook.
      • This shift involves automating the governance, security, evaluation, and API connectivity required to deploy thousands of autonomous agents safely.

Notable Quotes

"In a way, it's like this story of planting grass before building roads. You plant the grass, see where the flows [are], where people choose to go, and then you build the road in that path." — Antonio Bravo, on driving organic, employee-led AI adoption.
"If we build agents and then our employees don't use them because they are scared or fear what they might imply, we're not going to be successful. We have to have a positive narrative that actually is true and demonstrated to the employees." — Antonio Bravo, on change management.
"Our organization is not fit or designed to onboard agents... What I'm more excited about now is: how do we come up with this notion of industrialization of our agentic building capabilities so as to scale beyond what we're doing here to the rest of the processes in the bank?" — Antonio Bravo, on the future of BBVA's AI strategy.

The other pillar is managing across all this network of agents that we are starting to build. These are realization moments that show the way and prove the impact. Here, we are also creating a little bit of a snowball effect in terms of adoption because everyone wants to chip in. We have a team that manages AI adoption across the organization, both globally and in the individual countries. And basically, they do a lot of training. When you introduce new, powerful tools like ChatGPT, you need to accompany people. You can't think it's going to work by only deploying the product. How Meesho Became India’s Biggest Shopping App

Y Combinator(2.3M)·30:21·6.0K views·2026-06-11
Vidit AatreyCo-Founder & CEO, Meesho Ankit GuptaGeneral Partner, Y Combinator
TL;DRMeesho became India's dominant Android shopping app by relentlessly focusing on mass-market consumers and pivoting its business model through five distinct iterations—transitioning from a localized directory to a WhatsApp social commerce tool, and…
finally to a...

TL;DR

Meesho became India's dominant Android shopping app by relentlessly focusing on mass-market consumers and pivoting its business model through five distinct iterations—transitioning from a localized directory to a WhatsApp social commerce tool, and finally to a direct-to-consumer e-commerce giant processing 2.5 billion annual orders.

Meesho's Scale and Market Dominance

  • Massive Transaction Volumes: Meesho currently processes 2.5 billion orders per year, with its user base averaging 10 purchases annually.
  • Unrivaled User Base: The platform has 250 million unique annual buyers (growing at over 30% year-over-year) and close to 1 million active sellers.
  • Market Share: Out of India's estimated 350 to 400 million annual online shoppers, more than half purchase on Meesho.
  • App Store Leadership: Since launching its direct-to-consumer app on July 5, 2021, Meesho has held the #1 position in the shopping section of India's Android Play Store every single day.

The Journey of Iterations: From FashNear to Meesho Supply

  • The Core Thesis: Co-founders Vidit Aatrey and Sanjeev Barnwal launched the company in 2015 to bring e-commerce to "mass India" outside of major tech hubs like Bangalore, where online shopping was virtually non-existent.
  • Version 1 (FashNear): A localized directory to help physical fashion shops sell to nearby consumers.
    • *The Failure:* The team shut it down in three months after realizing they had only spoken to sellers and ignored the consumer experience, which ended up offering the worst of both physical malls and online shopping.
  • Version 2 (Meesho - "Meri Shop"): Developed during their YC Summer 2016 batch, this software helped physical shopkeepers manage inventory and collect payments over WhatsApp groups.
    • *The Monetization Bottleneck:* While hundreds of thousands of shops used it, local Indian businesses were unwilling to pay for software.
  • Version 3 (Meesho Supply / Product-Market Fit): The founders noticed "power users" who were online-native drop-shippers (mostly homemakers looking for zero-upfront-cost businesses). They launched a separate app to connect these resellers with wholesale suppliers.
    • *Organic Explosion:* With zero spend on marketing due to extreme capital constraints, Meesho Supply doubled in transaction volume every single month for 10 consecutive months, prompting the founders to kill the original app and rebrand.

The High-Stakes D2C Pivot in 2021

  • The Paradigm Shift: By 2020, cheap mobile data (driven by Reliance Jio) and the COVID-19 pandemic forced mass-market Indian consumers to learn how to shop online directly, making the data-saving, WhatsApp-mediated model obsolete.
  • The Hard Decision: Despite having 10 million active entrepreneurs running WhatsApp groups on their platform, Vidit and his team realized they had to bypass these intermediaries and go direct-to-consumer (D2C).
  • The Boardroom Risk: Investors were hesitant and wanted a slow experiment, but Vidit committed fully, knowing a half-hearted pivot would alienate sellers without winning over consumers.
  • The Result: Meesho launched its consumer app in July 2021. Within five months, it scaled from 10 million users to 100 million Monthly Active Users (MAUs). Competitors who failed to pivot quickly went out of business or were acquired.

The Next Frontier: AI-Driven "Invisible" Commerce

  • Targeting the Next 750 Million: To expand from 250 million users to 1 billion, Meesho must onboard rural demographics who struggle with digital literacy, app navigation, and written search.
  • Voice-First Shopping with "Vani": Meesho recently launched a voice AI agent named *Vani* designed to eliminate the need to read, type, or click buttons.
    • *The Workflow:* Consumers navigate the platform entirely through voice prompts and sliding images, speaking their address and OTP verbally to complete orders.
  • The Strategy: The company prioritizes long-term consumer accessibility over legacy software patterns. Vidit predicts that within a few years, e-commerce may not even require a traditional app interface, but rather a ubiquitous, conversational voice agent.

Notable Quotes

"Be very rigid with your problem and be very flexible with your solution. Even though from day one we started with the goal of bringing all Indian consumers and businesses online... the solutions keep changing, but you have to be very committed to the problem." — Vidit Aatrey, Co-founder and CEO of Meesho
"Unless you see product-market fit, you never know what product-market fit is... [It's] people coming to your app and they keep complaining you don't have this feature, you don't have that feature, and they still use your app like 15 or 20 times a day because you're solving such a core pain point." — Vidit Aatrey, Co-founder and CEO of Meesho
"It's hard to kill your existing business and start a new one. We did it... If we had not done it, I don't think we would be here today. The business would not exist." — Vidit Aatrey, Co-founder and CEO of Meesho

Both Sanjeev and I come from small towns. I was born in Meerut. Most of my family comes from Western UP, mostly a farmer family. Sanjeev was born and brought up in Hazaribagh in Jharkhand. Back in 2015, when we were trying to figure things out, first of all, we were very excited about this mobile internet wave. Both of us were two years out of college. We were like, "This is the time to build something." One thing we felt very passionate about was that back in 2015, if you were in Bangalore, you would see everyone around you buying stuff online. And then both of us would go back to our families in Meerut and Jharkhand, and you would find no one buying online, and no one selling online. Both of us would always talk about this all the time, feeling like there was something off. When you came to Bangalore, people felt that e-commerce had already happened. I think Flipkart got sold to Walmart back then, Amazon was there, and lots of other companies were there. People were like, "It's done. There's no new player who is going to come. What is there to solve?" Both of us basically believed there was definitely something needed because none of our families were buying online. So I think that was the simple reason why we started up. We said we have to bring everyone in India online. The beautiful thing is, 11 years forward, our business has evolved in many ways.
