Sanjeev used to work with Sony in Tokyo. I called him up and said, "Hey, I think we should start up now." There was so much stuff happening. Because I was part of an ad tech company, you could see all the startups in the Indian ecosystem who were spending budgets on this ad tech platform and growing really fast. I just felt that we had to do something, that the time was now. So I called him up. And so you kind of saw that from working at that first company—you saw that those budgets were going up real fast. Yes, I basically saw mobile internet taking off as part of that company. You could basically see everything change every month. Right. And so I called him up. He said, "Yeah, fine, makes sense." He resigned the next day, and one week later he came back. So he moved back from Tokyo? He was in Tokyo. Yes, and he was there for three years. And by the way, going back to our origin story even to college, that is the basic reason why he did that. Both of us have been close friends for a very long time. We were in the same department, the same hostel, the same wing. We were next to each other for all four years of our college life. We did a lot of things together. The day I called him up, he said, "Yeah, I've been thinking things are quite slow here and I want to do something more dynamic and faster. I've been here for three years now, and this looks great. I will come back and we'll try something." So I think that was basically the story. As I said, we landed on the idea that we really want to bring everyone in India—be it all consumers or businesses—into the digital ecosystem. But how do you basically get started with your first product? We decided to just go and speak to a few people. We asked, "Who is not selling online?" Mostly small businesses were not selling online. Brands were selling online, but small businesses—who were the largest part of the economy in India, representing 85% of GDP back then—were not. So we started to go and speak to a lot of these small businesses in Bangalore, in HSR, and in Koramangala. We would just go into these shops and ask them, "Hey, why are you not selling online? Why are you not selling online?" And everyone would give you a reason. So these are like regular shops—you just showed up at the shops? Regular shops across categories, yes. And we realized that all these shops said, "Hey, I want to sell online, but I don't know how to find my customers online. The people who buy from me are within a certain radius of the store. I don't have a unique differentiation beyond that, but these people really like my service and my curation, so I really want to sell to them." Sanjeev and I basically thought, "Hey, if we help this person sell to customers in this area, it'll be a great business." So we called our first version FashNear, standing for Fashion Nearby. As a fashion shop, you could list your shop here and sell to customers locally. We started that version, and three months later, we shut it down. In three months? Yes, and we had a very, very stupid learning. Our learning was that we started this product and we never, ever spoke to consumers. We just spoke to sellers. We thought the problem to solve was what the small businesses told us, but we never spoke to consumers because they were not buying online. So you only understood one side of the market really well, basically. It's like the most novice mistake you can make, yeah. Because when we started to push our app and ask consumers to buy, they would say, "This is the worst of both worlds. When I go to a mall, I can touch and feel the product, and try it on. When I go online, I get all the selection. You are giving me a limited selection of people in my locality, and I can't even touch and feel the product." So it was worse than a mall and worse than e-commerce. And we said, "Yeah, that makes sense. It's a horrible thing to do." We should have done this before, but I think the journey has been like this. Sometimes you have a cool learning, and sometimes you have these stupid learnings, but you keep evolving your product. I feel good about one thing: we did not spend a year or two on that idea. We basically shut it down in three months. We built our app, got hundreds of customers to try the product, and we very soon realized it didn't work. We shut it down and moved to the next version. And at this point, had you already gotten into YC, or did that happen later? YC happened actually a year after we started up. The second product was called Meesho, but it was different from what we eventually got product-market fit with. The product we started then was based on realizing that this whole local thing doesn't work, so let's tackle a different problem. We went back to these small businesses, and we said this time we will not ask them questions; we will basically sit in these shops and observe what the problems are. We wanted to observe a lot more than just speaking to them, because most of them articulate problems based on their own understanding, which may not point to the right solution. So, we would just go sit in these shops from morning to evening. Many of these people were really, really nice to us. We discovered a behavior where a lot of these shops, even though they would say they were not online, actually were online. They used to have a WhatsApp group. Whenever someone came and bought from a shop, at the end, they would take their phone number and say, "Hey, I will add you to a WhatsApp group and keep adding all the new products I have in my shop here." So they were basically selling—they had this very simplified version of an e-commerce flow where it's just a WhatsApp group they would send stuff on. Absolutely. It's easier to manage for them, and it's low friction for the consumer. Basically, the WhatsApp group was the online shop, yeah. We said, "This looks cool." We had been trying to get them into a standard shop format, but maybe in India, WhatsApp is going to be the shop, and we just have to make it better and solve the problems around it. We started to go deep and realized there were two or three big problems: collecting payments for stuff on WhatsApp, and updating inventory. Many times people would place an order on WhatsApp and, by then, the product was gone. So, that was Meesho. Meesho meant "Meri Shop," or "My Shop." We wanted to give them a shop. That's the first product we built, and we went to YC with it. Our goal was to make every business in India an online shop on WhatsApp. We did that for a year, went to YC, and came back. Then, we were short of money and could not raise our next round. The problem in India was that not a lot of small businesses were willing to pay you for software. Even though they were using our software to run their shop on WhatsApp, manage payments, and do all of this, they were like, "Hey, no. If you charge me for this, maybe I can just manage the way I was doing it earlier." So we realized we couldn't make real revenue in this business. What do we do? At the same time, we had close to hundreds of thousands of users using our product—hundreds of thousands of shops. And there was a certain segment of small business behavior... Wait, so you were already at hundreds of thousands of people using it? Hundreds of thousands of shops and users at this point? Yes, using it to run their business on WhatsApp. So, one way to look at that would have been, "Oh, we already have product-market fit; hundreds of thousands of people want it." But you're saying that you couldn't scale it up another 10x, basically, because even at that point, not enough people were willing to pay for it to do that. Yes, that's true. I would say there were hundreds of thousands of people who were doing this, but they were not the most active. That is where the insight comes in: we tried to find the people who were the most active here, and we found a very different profile of businesses that were the most active on our product. These were online-native businesses. They didn't have an offline shop, and they were using our product to sell on WhatsApp groups only. Oh, interesting. So they were drop-shippers. They were drop-shippers. In our language, they called themselves resellers. They were drop-shippers, and they were using our product day in and day out. Whereas, when you look at an offline person, they would use it for a few days because it looks good to have an offline shop, but then, who is going to update inventory every day or collect payments? They would just revert back to old behaviors because using an online tool and making that big change can feel overwhelming. If their online business is a small share of their overall revenue, why would they change? But all of these users who were online-native were the power users of our product. Okay, so you found people who really loved it. Yes. So we said, "We have to make money from them." No one else was going to pay us money, so we had to make money from them. Sanjeev and I started doing the same thing: we went to their houses, spread across Bangalore and sometimes outside, and asked them to show us how they ran their businesses so we could figure out the problems. The biggest problem we figured out was that most of them were women—homemakers who wanted to start a business of their own. The reason they became drop-shippers was because drop-shipping is a zero upfront cost business. You don't have to buy inventory and sell; you just connect with suppliers who directly ship to consumers. We realized their biggest problem was getting access to these suppliers and supply, so we asked ourselves, "Can we build that product?" We built a new app called Meesho Supply, listed as a separate app on the Play Store. Through it, these users could get access to supply from us and start selling. We saw that product grow like nothing we had ever seen before. I think that is when I understood what PMF really means. Unless you actually see product-market fit, you never know what it is. Can you tell us a little bit about what that felt like, and share whatever metrics you feel comfortable sharing? Yes. For the next 10 months, we spent zero rupees on marketing because we did not have money. As I said, money was our biggest constraint. We didn't have money until we got to our Series A, yet we doubled every single month. Every month you doubled? Wow. Through organic discovery and very high retention. It was like people coming to your app, complaining that you don't have this feature or that feature, yet still using your app 15 or 20 times a day. Because you are solving such a core pain point for them, they are investing their own time in improving your product and giving you feedback. So Sanjeev and I said, "This looks like product-market fit, like something we have never seen before." We shut down our first app called Meesho, rebranded the Meesho Supply app into Meesho, and basically that became our product. It was a social commerce product where anyone could become a Meesho entrepreneur or drop-shipper, run a WhatsApp group, and sell products to consumers. So, your first evolution was from being a toolkit for sellers to basically a full supplier for sellers. You were giving them a business in a box, essentially built on WhatsApp. Okay, so this is now like 2019 or 2020, right? Something like that. I recall you telling me that once COVID happened, a few big things changed. Tell us a little bit about what this was like at the peak of this product, and then what happened in the few years after that. Yes. If I zoom out just to paint the big picture so you understand why this was happening: back in 2016, the cost of cellular data on your phone in India was very high. Yes. And all shopping apps are image-heavy and consume a lot of data. If you went online and started browsing, you would keep consuming data, and you would keep getting messages from Airtel or Vodafone saying "one rupee deducted" or "50 paisa deducted." A lot of consumers would just uninstall the app and never use it again because they felt it was consuming too much money. Whereas on WhatsApp, you decide when you want to download an image. Most of it is text, which is very efficient, and WhatsApp downsizes images to keep data consumption very low. So, a lot of people were buying on WhatsApp but would not download a standalone app. We realized you could go really deep into the country this way. People who were not buying anything online would want to try buying online if you helped them buy on WhatsApp. So WhatsApp had a real distribution advantage over anything else in this era. Absolutely. So we said, "Let's just go big on this." We got to this product in 2016, and for the next five years, we were committed to it. In peak 2020, we had 10 million WhatsApp groups in India running for the sole purpose of buying products from Meesho. Holy crap. So that's not just 10 million buyers. That's 10 million sellers—10 million entrepreneurs. 10 million Meesho entrepreneurs, absolutely. And they must have been selling to 100 million people or more. Yes, we believe that number was 100 million. But then, in 2020, we realized the same premise didn't hold anymore. The problem we were solving of data being expensive was gone. Jio happened, and the cost of data went to zero. With the pandemic, a lot of people were forced to learn how to buy online because they were stuck at home. We realized that if we didn't move consumers to our own app, we would eventually lose them to someone else. So, with this earth-shaking paradigm shift of data going to zero, not many people necessarily felt the massive impact of that. For most, it was just, "Oh great, data is cheaper." But for your business, that was a fundamental threat to your original model. Absolutely. Tell us what your psychology was in that moment. You had a business with 10 million sellers, many of whom were helping you make a lot of money. You were presumably highly successful. And then you decided, "Okay, I think we have to abandon this." How did you decide that, and what did you decide to do? It was a very interesting time period in the company, and on the board as well. We were already a unicorn then, we had raised a significant amount of money, and had a lot of investors on the board. They were like, "Can we do this as an experiment? Can we see it for a while?" I was actually clear that you can't do this as an experiment. The day you go directly to the consumer, all of those drop-shippers are going to hate you because you are disintermediating them. They feel their business is going to go away. If you do this as an experiment, it's a lose-lose; you won't get the consumers, you will lose the WhatsApp group owners, and you will have nothing left. You have to commit to this direction. The big reason we had conviction that this was the right thing to do was the same reason we had conviction before: we went and spoke to consumers. Sanjeev and I, and a lot of people in our team, started to go on the ground. We met consumers and understood that all the assumptions we previously had were going away. None of them had a problem downloading an app. None of them struggled with feeling like, "How do I buy?" or "What does add to cart mean?" While a majority of India still faced some of these barriers, much of it had gone away. We were very clear that if you take a long-term view, you have to do it. There has never been a better time. The good thing was this was the peak of capital availability in the startup ecosystem. There were people willing to provide us capital to make this shift, because going direct-to-consumer and building awareness for an app takes a lot of money. We said, "It's tough, but we will do it." I was very fortunate that we had the right team and the right investors who took the right long-term view and said, "This makes sense. This will be a much larger opportunity if we do this. It's a bit risky, but if we don't do it, who else will?" Actually, there were a lot of other companies that had a similar product to us back in 2021, and none of them ever pivoted. None of them are known anymore; all of them have shut down, got acquired, or closed down. They had some kind of selling-on-WhatsApp type of thing. Yes, a lot of our clones had come up, so they were running that business. But as I said, it's hard. It's hard to kill your existing business and start a new one. We did it. I remember it was the mid of 2021—July 5th, 2021—when we launched our app. On July 7th, 2021, we went number one in the shopping section of the Android Play Store in India. Today is 2026. Every day since then, we've been the number one shopping app on the Android Play Store every single day. You will never go to Android and find anyone else. That's awesome. We used to have 10 million users for our app built for social sellers. Five months later, we had 100 million monthly active users. One hundred million consumers were coming to our app. That's why I believe about 100 million people were buying or at least viewing our products through this channel, because they all moved here in just five months. When you look back, you'll say that's the best decision we made. If we had not done it, I don't think we would be here today. The business would not exist. The reason we had conviction that we would not fail is because we were so close to our consumers. As a consumer company, that's the most important skill you need to have. You have to be the closest to your consumer. You need to know things that none of your competition or people in the ecosystem know about them so you can take contrarian bets that no one else will take. That's what we did, and it eventually worked out. Many things don't work out. It could have been that we did this and something went wrong, but fortunately, in this case, it worked out. That's why we have had many versions and an evolving product, but the same mission. Internally, we have a company value that says, "Be problem-first." Be very rigid with your problem and very flexible with your solution. Our journey has been exactly that. Even though the solutions keep changing, you have to be very committed to the problem you started with. I think the thing that really strikes me about this, which is a universal story of great companies that make it, is that to survive for 10-plus years and make it big, you have to survive at least a few big underlying changes in how the world works. Some big paradigm shift occurs every few years. For you guys, it was a shift that probably would have been very hard to predict—that all of a sudden, essentially overnight, the baseline assumption of how your business works changes. That customer obsession and focus allowed you guys to get through it. Now, to bring it to the obvious current moment, we're going through another big technology shift, a potentially disruptive moment with AI. I imagine a lot of people working in e-commerce see it as very disruptive, and I imagine you do as well. How do you think about what that means for your business, whether there will be another huge change ahead, or whether you can leverage it to your advantage? You are absolutely right. I believe AI is going to change how you run shopping online, e-commerce, and logistics; every single thing will change. We are essentially a software company, and 70% of our headcount is in tech and product, which is changing big time with AI. I think this is the same '21 moment for us, and that's what we keep reminding ourselves internally. We always say that all our good decisions have been focused on the long term while managing the short term, and it has to be the same thing now. We have to say, "Forget what we have today. If you were starting an e-commerce app with this mission statement using AI, what would you do?" Actually, we are trying to do that. For example, we recently launched a new voice agent called Vani. To take a step back and explain why: most of our innovations over the last 11 years have been around two axes. One is accessibility: removing barriers so more people try a product. WhatsApp was in that direction, and recently we've gone into content as a way to transact to reduce friction. The other axis of innovation has been affordability. If you want a billion people to buy, they all struggle with doing more with less budget. We've solved that with a different kind of logistics and a different way of running e-commerce. That is why people trust us with their purchases across categories. Accessibility, I feel, is going to change again with AI. Today, we have 250 million people buying from us every year, not a billion. I keep saying that getting from 250 million to a billion is most likely going to happen with AI. When you go to rural areas in India and ask people to download an app and try it, they cannot use it. They don't understand what is written, even in their own language, and they don't know what "add to cart" means because there is no offline equivalent. They don't understand ratings or reviews, and they are afraid that clicking a button will deduct money from their bank accounts. It is extremely overwhelming and tough for them. That is a large part of who we have to serve going forward. When we started testing this voice AI product, we said we wanted to build an experience where a consumer never has to read anything on the app, never has to type anything, and never has to click a button. So the software just becomes invisible. Yes, you just have voice, and images keep coming in front of you and going away. You keep saying, "Yes, I like this" or "I don't like this." Then you verbally say your address, provide your OTP to transact, and move on. You never do any of the things that feel overwhelming. That is the vision now: can you use AI to create experiences on the consumer side, the seller side, and the logistics side that reduce the barrier to entry so much that everyone comes forward? We are paranoid about this; we have to do this before anyone else does, because no one else should get to a billion people before us. I believe this is one way the business will change, and the product could look very different. Soon we may not even have an app—it could just be a voice agent that you use to transact. All businesses will change in a big way soon, and you have to let go of past baggage. What you have today doesn't matter. You have to take a long-term view and do the right thing, even if it is disruptive in the short term. That is the plan. The thing that always sticks with me is how much you think about the customer as the starting point of your decision-making. You could have answered that question from the standpoint of "this technology is now possible, so let's roll it out," versus starting with what the customer's pain is. I think that is such a timeless lesson, and it's what allowed you to successfully navigate all your pivots to get where you are today. To wrap up, if you were sitting in the audience here as a college student or a young technical person, much like you were when you started your company, what pieces of parting advice would you give them? What things did you do in 2015 and 2016 that are most relevant today, especially in this new technological moment? What should they be doing? I would say this feels like the 2015–16 of the new paradigm. Back then, mobile internet was taking off, and Sanjeev and I got excited and jumped on. I think there has never been a better time than right now. AI is very early. Everyone is unclear about what is going to happen two or three years down the line, so you can have a point of view and jump in. If I were starting today, I would do the exact same thing I did in 2015 and 2016. I would see what people are doing around me, and then I would go back to my hometown and see what people are doing there to find what is not happening. I think all of those gaps can now be bridged with AI. You cannot say anymore that people are not using a tool because they don't understand it, are not tech-savvy, or don't find it useful. Everything can be reimagined, especially for a country like India where people often find technology overwhelming. So, I would do the exact same thing, but the output would be very different. That's incredible. I think that is a very inspiring message to leave people with. Thank you so much for joining us, Vidit. Appreciate it.

ECB Governing Council Press Conference - 11 June 2026

European Central Bank(104.0K)·50:07·22.2K views·2026-06-11
Christine LagardePresident, European Central Bank
TL;DRThe ECB Governing Council unanimously raised its three key interest rates by 25 basis points in response to persistent inflationary pressures driven by the Middle East conflict, maintaining a highly data-dependent, meeting-by-meeting policy approach without pre-committing to a specific rate path.

TL;DR

The ECB Governing Council unanimously raised its three key interest rates by 25 basis points in response to persistent inflationary pressures driven by the Middle East conflict, maintaining a highly data-dependent, meeting-by-meeting policy approach without pre-committing to a specific rate path.

Monetary Policy Action and Inflation Outlook

  • Interest Rate Decision: The ECB raised its three key interest rates by 25 basis points. The decision was unanimous and robust across all analyzed economic scenarios, including adverse, severe, and a newly introduced "milder" scenario.
  • Revised Inflation Projections: Staff upwardly revised the baseline inflation outlook, primarily driven by a higher path for energy prices:
      • Headline Inflation: Expected to average 3.0% in 2026, 2.3% in 2027, and 2.0% in 2028.
      • Core Inflation (ex-energy/food): Projected to average 2.5% in 2026 and 2027, before easing to 2.2% in 2028.
  • Current Price Pressures: Inflation rose to 3.2% in May (up from 3.0% in April). Energy inflation ticked up to 10.9% in April, while services inflation increased significantly from 3.0% to 3.5%.
  • Inflation Expectations: Shorter-term inflation expectations remain elevated and well above pre-war levels. However, longer-term expectations remain anchored around the 2.0% target.
  • Policy Characterization: ECB President Christine Lagarde rejected the label of an "insurance" or "preemptive" rate hike, clarifying that it is a direct, sensible monetary policy response to an enduring energy shock.

Economic Growth and Activity

  • Revised GDP Projections: Growth baseline projections were revised downward for 2026 and 2027, reflecting the drag of energy costs and geopolitical uncertainty on confidence and real incomes:
      • Growth Outlook: Baseline GDP growth is projected at 0.8% in 2026, 1.2% in 2027, and 1.5% in 2028.
  • Labor Market Resilience: The labor market remains historically tight, with the unemployment rate at 6.3% in April. While job creation continues, labor demand has cooled, and firms expect the labor market to soften ahead.
  • Sectoral Divergence:
      • Services: Showing signs of a slowdown as the war in the Middle East weighs on consumer confidence.
      • Manufacturing: Holding up due to firms building safety stocks to handle supply chain pressures and increased public defense spending.
  • Domestic Demand: Solid household balance sheets and positive wage growth mean consumption is expected to remain the primary driver of growth in the medium term.

Macroeconomic Risks and Financial Stability

  • Downside Growth Risks: Growth risks are tilted to the downside, primarily due to the war in the Middle East. Prolonged energy disruptions, the closure of shipping routes, tighter credit conditions, and Russia's ongoing war in Ukraine remain significant threats.
  • Upside Inflation Risks: Inflation risks are heavily tilted to the upside, tied to prolonged high energy prices, broader wage-price pass-through, trade fragmentation, and extreme weather events driving up food prices.
  • Banking Sector Health: Euro area banks remain highly resilient with strong capital, solid liquidity ratios, and robust profitability.
  • Financial Stability Vulnerabilities: The primary risks to financial stability include sudden asset price corrections—amplified by the non-bank financial sector—and deteriorating asset quality in energy and trade-sensitive industries.

Fiscal Guidelines, Structural Reforms, and Emerging Tech Risks

  • Fiscal Policy Stance: The ECB urged member states to maintain sound public finances. Fiscal support measures addressing the energy shock must be temporary, targeted, and tailored to preserve fiscal sustainability.
  • Structural Reforms: There is an urgent need to fast-track the savings and investment union, enhance single-market integration, and accelerate the transition away from fossil fuels to boost productivity and strategic autonomy.
  • The Digital Euro: Swift adoption of the regulation on the digital euro is deemed essential to enhance Europe's payment innovations and competitiveness.
  • Artificial Intelligence (AI) Risks: The ECB is actively monitoring systemic and cybersecurity risks posed by rapid AI developments. The central bank is taking defensive patching measures to prevent hacking and is working with the Single Supervisory Mechanism (SSM) to ensure commercial banks safeguard themselves against AI-driven intrusions.

Notable Quotes

"The Governing Council is committed to setting monetary policy to ensure that inflation stabilizes at our 2% target in the medium term. In line with this commitment, we today decided to raise the three key ECB interest rates by 25 basis points." — Christine Lagarde, ECB President
"We will closely monitor the situation and follow a data-dependent and meeting-by-meeting approach to determining the appropriate monetary policy stance... We are not pre-committing to a particular rate path." — Christine Lagarde, ECB President
"Our discussion was predicated on obviously the major energy shock that we have observed since the beginning of March that is enduring longer than what was expected... It is a monetary policy decision that stands." — Christine Lagarde, ECB President
Good afternoon. Welcome to our press conference. I'm joined on stage by President Lagarde and Vice-President Vujčić. My name is Wolfgang Preussl. I also welcome the journalists who are following us online. If they ask a question, I would ask them to turn on their cameras and put on their microphones. And with that, I would like to hand over to President Lagarde, please. Well, thank you very much, Wolfgang, and good afternoon to all of you. The Vice President—our new Vice President—and I welcome you to our press conference. The Governing Council is committed to setting monetary policy to ensure that inflation stabilizes at our 2% target in the medium term. In line with this commitment, we today decided to raise the three key ECB interest rates by 25 basis points. The war in the Middle East is generating inflationary pressures, and the decision to raise rates is robust across a range of scenarios mapping out how the shock might evolve and affect the medium-term outlook for the euro area. In the baseline of the new Eurosystem staff projections, headline inflation is expected to average 3% in 2026, 2.3% in 2027, and 2% in 2028. For inflation excluding energy and food, the baseline foresees an average of 2.5% in 2026 and 2027, and 2.2% in 2028. Compared with March, staff have revised up their baseline projection for inflation in 2026 and 2027 owing to a higher path for energy prices, which to some extent is expected to feed into food, goods, and services inflation. The baseline sees economic growth at an average of 0.8% in 2026, 1.2% in 2027, and 1.5% in 2028. This is a downward revision for 2026 and 2027, reflecting a more pronounced impact of the war on commodity markets, real incomes, and also confidence. The outlook remains uncertain, with upside risks for inflation and downside risks for economic growth. The full implication of the war for medium-term inflation and growth will depend on the intensity and duration of the energy price shock, as well as the scale of its indirect and second-round effects. This uncertainty is also reflected in the broad range of outcomes for inflation and growth in the updated illustrative scenarios put together by Eurosystem staff. These will be published with the staff projections on our website. With today's decision, we remain well-positioned to navigate the uncertainty caused by the war. We will closely monitor the situation and follow a data-dependent and meeting-by-meeting approach to determining the appropriate monetary policy stance. In particular, our interest rate decisions will be based on our assessment of the inflation outlook and the risks surrounding it in light of the incoming economic and financial data, as well as the dynamics of underlying inflation and the strength of monetary policy transmission. We are not pre-committing to a particular rate path. The decisions taken today are set out in a press release available on our website. So, I will now outline in more detail how we see the economy and inflation developing, and will then explain our assessment of financial and monetary conditions. Let me first look at economic activity. Adjusting for a temporary factor in Ireland, the euro area economy grew in the first quarter, supported by domestic demand and exports. Yet, the war in the Middle East is weighing on activity, and surveys are pointing to a slowdown, especially in services. Manufacturing has held up so far. In part, this is because firms have been building up stocks to cope with supply chain pressures. It also reflects higher defense spending. The labor market remains resilient, and unemployment, at 6.3% in April, remains close to historical lows. The first quarter saw additional jobs being created, although at a slower pace than in the last quarter of 2025. Labor demand has cooled further, and firms and households expect the labor market to weaken. Looking ahead, staff now expect domestic demand to be weaker than they projected in March as the war weighs on confidence and higher energy costs erode real incomes. At the same time, household balance sheets are solid overall, and consumption should remain the main driver of growth. Higher energy costs and lower confidence will dent private investment in the short run, but it should be underpinned by firms investing in new digital technologies. Government spending more on defense and infrastructure should continue to support public investments. These factors are expected to provide some cushioning against the fallout from the war. The Governing Council highlights the urgent need to strengthen the euro area economy while maintaining sound public finances. Fiscal sustainability is a crucial anchor for broader economic stability. Fiscal responses to the energy price shock should be temporary, targeted, and tailored, as emphasized in the European Commission's 2026 European Semester Spring Package. Reforms to enhance the euro area's growth potential and accelerate the energy transition to reduce reliance on fossil fuels are more vital than ever. Completing the savings and investment union is key to funding innovation, supporting the green and digital transition, and improving productivity. The digital euro and tokenized wholesale central bank money will enhance Europe's strategic autonomy, competitiveness, and financial integration, and will boost innovation in payments. It is thus essential to swiftly adopt the regulation on the establishment of the digital euro. Simplifying and harmonizing rules across the EU single market will help European firms grow faster. Let me now look at inflation. Inflation rose to 3.2% in May from 3% in April. Energy price inflation ticked up to 10.9% in April, while food price inflation fell from 2.4% to 2%. Inflation excluding energy and food picked up to 2.5% from 2.2% in April, as goods inflation edged up to 0.9% and services inflation increased from 3% to 3.5%. Domestic cost pressures eased in the first quarter, supported by slower growth in wages and profits. The ECB's wage tracker and surveys on wage expectations continue to indicate that wage growth should ease over the year. However, it is becoming more expensive for firms to source other inputs, and they therefore expect to put up their selling prices. Moreover, some indicators of underlying inflation have already been driven higher by the energy shock. Inflation expectations over shorter horizons remain well above levels before the outbreak of the war in the Middle East. At the same time, most measures of longer-term inflation expectations stand at around 2%, supporting the stabilization of inflation around our target in the medium term. The increase in energy prices will lift inflation further over the summer and keep it well above target into the first half of 2027. It will also have an impact on food, goods, and services inflation. Inflation should then return to target in the second half of 2027, supported by falling energy prices and slower increases in other prices. However, the war in the Middle East remains a major source of uncertainty, and the longer energy prices stay high, the more likely they are to drive up broader inflation through indirect and second-round effects. We will therefore closely monitor the size and persistence of the energy price increase and how it feeds through to price and wage setting, inflation expectations, and overall economic dynamics. Turning now to the risk assessment, the risks to the growth outlook are to the downside, mainly owing to the war in the Middle East, which has added to the volatile global policy environment. Prolonged disruption of energy supplies could increase energy prices further and for longer than currently expected. These factors would erode real incomes even more and make firms and households more reluctant to invest and spend. The drag on growth would intensify if the closure of major shipping routes were to cause acute shortages of key inputs that forced euro area firms to curtail output. A worsening of global financial market sentiment or a tighter supply of credit could dampen demand. Additional frictions in international trade could also further disrupt supply chains, reduce exports, and weaken consumption and investment. Other geopolitical tensions—in particular, Russia's unjustified war against Ukraine—remain a major source of uncertainty. By contrast, growth could turn out to be higher if the economy and energy markets were to adapt more quickly than expected to the disruption caused by the war in the Middle East, or if the war was resolved promptly and sustainably. Moreover, planned defense and infrastructure spending, reforms to enhance productivity, and euro area firms adopting new technologies may drive up growth by more than expected. A deeper integration of the single market would also boost growth beyond current expectations. The risks now to the inflation outlook are to the upside. If energy prices were to rise by more and for longer than currently expected, euro area inflation would increase further. This could be reinforced and become more persistent if higher energy prices were to spill over by more than expected to other prices and to wages, if longer-term inflation expectations were to rise in response, or if global supply chains were disrupted more broadly. Ongoing trade tensions could also give rise to more fragmented global supply chains, curtail the supply of critical raw materials, and worsen capacity constraints in the euro area economy. Extreme weather events and the unfolding climate and nature crisis more broadly could drive up food prices by more than expected. By contrast, inflation could turn out to be somewhat lower if the economic effects of the war in the Middle East proved to be more short-lived than currently expected, or if indirect or second-round effects proved less pronounced than anticipated. More volatile and risk-averse financial markets could weigh on demand and thereby lower inflation as well. So, looking now at financial and monetary conditions, financial conditions are broadly unchanged since our last meeting but remain tighter than before the war. The cost of issuing market-based debt rose to 4% in April from 3.9% in March. Bank lending rates for firms remained at 3.6% in April, and mortgage rates were at 3.4%. The annual growth rate of bank lending to firms increased to 3.4% in April from 3.2% in March, while the growth rate of corporate bond issuance rose to 4.6%. Mortgage lending in April again grew by 3%. In line with our monetary policy strategy, the Governing Council thoroughly assessed the links between monetary policy and financial stability. Euro area banks are resilient, supported by strong capital and liquidity ratios, solid asset quality, and robust profitability. However, a sudden sharp drop in asset prices—potentially amplified by the non-bank financial sector—and deteriorating asset quality, particularly in energy and trade-sensitive sectors, would pose risks to financial stability. These risks increase the longer the current geopolitical conflict lasts. Macroprudential policy remains the first line of defense against the buildup of financial vulnerabilities, enhancing resilience and preserving macroprudential space. So, in conclusion, the Governing Council today decided to raise the three key ECB interest rates by 25 basis points. We are committed to setting monetary policy to ensure that inflation stabilizes at our 2% target in the medium term. We will follow a data-dependent and meeting-by-meeting approach to determining the appropriate monetary policy stance. Our interest rate decisions will be based on our assessment of the inflation outlook and the risks surrounding it in light of the incoming economic and financial data, as well as the dynamics of underlying inflation and the strength of monetary policy transmission. We are not pre-committing to a particular rate path. In any case, we stand ready to adjust all of our instruments within our mandate to ensure that inflation stabilizes sustainably at our medium-term target and to preserve the smooth functioning of monetary policy transmission. And we now stand ready to take your questions. The first question today goes to Balazs Koranyi of Reuters. Balazs, please. Good afternoon. First off, allow me to say a big hello to the newest member of the trio on stage, Boris. President Lagarde, could you give us a flavor of the debate today? Were other options discussed—perhaps a hold, perhaps a larger increase? What were the arguments put forth in the discussion? The second question is about your reaction function. I'm curious how much, how many more rate increases are embedded into the staff projections? What do you need to see to deliver on those, and will you have that sort of volume and quality of data available to you by July to make an informed decision? Thank you very much for your question, and thank you for greeting the three of us and particularly Boris, our new Vice President. So, the decision that we took today to raise by 25 basis points our three interest rates was a unanimous decision without reservations. We did not discuss or debate any other alternative proposal, and the one that was predicated on the Eurosystem staff projections and the recommendations of our Chief Economist was unanimously endorsed. There was no discussion of others. Our reaction function has been very steady, repeated over the course of time, and I hope I will not offend you by repeating yet again that given the level of uncertainty that we are facing, we will decide on a meeting-by-meeting basis. We will be data-dependent. There will be no preset rate path in our considerations, and as a result of that, it will be what it will be. Now, this is not much by way of forward guidance, but as you know, under the present circumstances, there is no such justification for the decision that we are making and that we will make in the future. I think I would add one thing, because I've read here and there that the ECB will make an "insurance" decision—it will be an insurance matter, it will be a preemptive rate decision. It's not at all the way we had our discussion, really. Our discussions were predicated on, obviously, the major energy shock that we have observed since the beginning of March that is enduring longer than what was expected by geopolitical experts, and which we are beginning to see broadening throughout the economy, with direct costs being obvious, with indirect costs also showing up. As I said, we will be monitoring attentively any further consequences of this major energy shock. My second response is in relation to this insurance allegation of ours, which it was not. We have updated our scenarios, but if you remember from last time around, we had two scenarios which we published. We will be publishing a third scenario that will be available on our website, which we have decided to call the milder scenario. So, staff from the Eurosystem have worked on the update of the adverse and the severe scenarios, but they have also worked on a milder scenario in order to be on both sides of the baseline. And the decision that we took today to increase by 25 basis points all our interest rates is robust across the three scenarios—the two updated adverse and severe, but also the milder scenario, which obviously is more positive and something that we should not neglect, but which is at this point in time unlikely to materialize. But we still thought that it was our duty to look at both sides, and on all accounts our decision stands. If we were not taking that very obvious monetary policy decision, then at the end of the medium term that we look out for projection purposes, we would be north of our target. So, I hope I have covered your question. Next question to Annette Weisbach of CNBC. Annette, please. Thank you very much, Madame Lagarde. I have two questions, obviously. I would like to ask you about the recent uptick in core inflation. We have seen that service prices are on the rise, so is that, in your opinion, already a sign that we are seeing the first signs of second-round effects? And then my second question would be: where are we in between all these scenarios? Are we closer to the adverse camp? Are we approaching the severe camp? What's the opinion of the ECB? Thank you very much. Well, on the services inflation, as you will have seen, it has moved from 3% to 3.5%, which is a significant increase. Now, obviously, what we need to understand better is what is under the skin of that increase, and how we can distinguish between what is the direct and indirect consequence of the significant increase in energy prices, or whether there is something else. At this point in time, we cannot yet say whether this significant increase in service prices is attributable exclusively to direct, or to direct and indirect effects. What we are confident in, though, is that we are not seeing yet—we are not seeing yet—second-round effects, which would be largely attributable to wages, which play a significant contribution in the production of services. But I'm saying again that we will be monitoring closely all that in the months to come. Now, you asked me where we stand, and I just want to take you back to a speech that I gave back in March, which I know you've been following. It was my speech to the ECB Watchers. And in March, at the ECB Watchers conference, I distinguished between three different situations, which you know are an analytical frame in and of itself, if you will, so I'm not going to consider that one is exactly scenario X, Y, or Z. But I said, first of all, if the shock is limited and short-lived, it's a case where we see through. Now, obviously, we do not find ourselves in this situation because we have decided, and we think, that it's a monetary policy that stands firmly on its own and that is robust across all scenarios, but we are not in that sort of "let's see through, it's going to be short-lived and small" situation. This is not the case. The second situation that I referred to is the situation where the shock produces a large, though not too persistent, overshoot, in which case a measured adjustment of policy is needed. Okay. The third case that I referred to in that ECB Watchers speech is the situation where we expect inflation to deviate significantly and durably from target, in which case the response must be appropriately forceful. So, I leave it to you to decide which of the three. I've already eliminated case number one, which is clearly not a situation where it's short-lived and rather small. This is not the case. The decision we've made is not a forceful decision, either, right? A 25 basis points increase is a decision which clearly is a signal and is necessary, given the economic situation that we have, given the uncertainty that we are navigating, given the inflation outlook that we have, and the projections that have been produced by the Eurosystem staff. Next question, Alex Weber of Bloomberg News. Alex, please. Good afternoon. Thanks very much. On the sort of measured adjustment that you might be doing, does the neutral range play a role in your thinking in that regard? Is 2.5% for the deposit rate an important threshold, or could a measured adjustment also mean a slight step into restrictive territory? And then secondly, before today, there were quite a few economists who warned that a rate hike in the current environment would be a mistake. Did you reflect on these comments, and what's your response to this? So, at the risk of disappointing you, we have not discussed the neutral rate, nor the range of the neutral rate. As you know, there is a lot of work that has been done by staff in that respect, which has given a range within which the neutral rate would be located. Philip Lane has discussed that extensively in a speech recently, but we have not debated our positioning with reference to the neutral rate. This rate, I remind you, is a bit difficult to actually assess precisely, because we are not in a situation where there are no shocks, which is typically what you need to have as the sort of environment within which you determine where you are neutral, because you don't stimulate and you don't restrict. So, this was not debated at all by the Governing Council. So, you know, everybody has to do what they have to do. Our job is price stability, and our job is to apply the strategic review principles that we have agreed. Our job is to follow carefully the reaction function that you are all familiar with, and that we try to be as transparent about as possible so that we are predictable. In the face of that, frankly, given the numbers we have, given the inflation outlook, given the risks to the upside on inflation, and given the robustness of the decision against and across all scenarios—the bad one, the very bad one, and the not-so-bad one—the fact that it holds in all circumstances is clear. And if you add to that the fact that our growth forecast, as revised, is 0.8%—you know, minus 0.1% relative to March—and then 1.2% next year and 1.5% the following year, it's not as if we are in an environment where growth is absent or under significant threat. A lot more can be done by structural reforms, by encouraging a European market that has no obstacles to trade, a savings and investment union, and so on and so forth, and we encourage that very strongly in our monetary policy statement. Thank you. And now Olaf Storbeck of the Financial Times. Olaf, please. Hi. I have one question on the inflation forecast. What struck my eye, when I compared the current baseline with the March adverse scenario, is that headline inflation is lower than in the adverse scenario, but the core inflation is higher for '26. Does this mean that kind of spillover effects are already bigger than you anticipated in March, and what is driving them? And my second question is: you clearly said this rate hike isn't an insurance hike. What is it then? Is it the start of a new hiking cycle, or what else? Thanks. Well, thank you very much for your two questions. So, with reference to the baseline, first of all, I have not compared, and we have not gone into the exercise of comparing, the baseline with the adverse or the severe scenario at this point in time. We have updated our baseline both on the account of inflation and on the account of growth as well, with the caveat of Ireland, which is, you know, a case in point of international companies organizing their business in such a way that it impacts the Irish numbers. But so, what we have done is that we've updated the baseline, and I think the best thing that can be done is to compare the adverse scenario prepared in March with the adverse scenario compared in June. I think that is more relevant than comparing the baseline versus last time around's adverse scenario. And as I told you in the earlier phase of our discussions today, we are beginning to see a broadening of inflation throughout the economy, and that is obviously in terms of direct effects but also in terms of indirect effects. We are not yet, at this point, in front of second-round effects, but we are going to be extremely attentive. When we see, for instance, that delivery dates or the smoothness of supply is beginning to be impaired, obviously we will be attentive to those sorts of ancillary indicators that would help us understand where it is heading. One more point, because I did speak about wages earlier: we do include a level of second-round effects in our projections, obviously. The projections that we have for all three years, actually—'26, '27, and '28—all anticipate compensation per employee at 3.2%, which in and of itself, if you compare that with the projection we have on inflation, justifies the fact that the net income of workers will actually be positive. This would lead us to assume that consumption will not only continue to be the main driver of growth, as it is, but will continue to grow, probably more than other factors. We see other factors, too, fueling growth going forward. Now, yes, I did say that it's not an "insurance policy" interest rate decision, simply because it's a good monetary policy interest rate decision. When you have the latest reading at 3.2%, when you have the inflation outlook over our target peaking in the end of '26, continuing to be over target throughout most of '27, returning to 2% in the autumn of '27, and being at target in '28 on the basis of market curves and all the rest of it, it is pretty obvious that we have to make a decision—that it is a sensible monetary policy decision. So, I don't need to characterize it as credibility, insurance, or anything else, for that matter. It's a monetary policy decision that stands, and that is, as we say in the monetary policy statement, robust across all scenarios, including the mild one. Moving to the other side of the room, Marina Ninkovic of the Croatian Broadcaster. Welcome. Thank you. Hello, I have a question for Madame Lagarde. Croatia is one of the newest members of the euro area, yet for many months it continues to experience a rather high inflation rate, way above the EU average. What do you have to say to the citizens who are concerned about this? Thank you. Well, thank you very much for your question. We are, of course, delighted that Croatia is a member of the euro area. I and all Governing Council members, as well as members of the Executive Board, are delighted to have the Croatian National Bank's former Governor now serving as a member of the Executive Board and as the new Vice President of the ECB. It's a great representation, it brings diversity to the group, and it's really a blessing that Boris has joined us. But turning to inflation, of course, we are concerned about inflation, but we are not just concerned about inflation in one member state or another, because our job at the ECB is to look at the whole of the euro area. Our concern, as I said earlier on, is that we see energy price inflation, which is at 10.9% at the moment, broadening throughout the economy. Which is why we are making this decision today. The broadening inflation outlook, underlying inflation—all of that points in the direction of having to make a decision to increase interest rates. So, because our mission and our mandate is to restore price stability, we make those decisions. And we are driven by that objective: price stability. Thank you. And now Elisa Piazza of Class CNBC, Italian Broadcaster. Thank you for taking my question. Well, you do not say anymore in your statement that longer-term inflation expectations remain well anchored. So, if you could tell us a little bit more about this change. And then I have another question on another topic. We know that the ECB has convened banks to discuss the risks associated with the new AI models, such as Mythos. So, what actions is the ECB taking on this issue, and do you consider these models to pose a potential systemic risk to the European banking sector? Thank you. Thank you so much for your two questions. So, in terms of inflation expectations, we look at three categories of expectations. We look at market-based measures, we look at survey-based measures, and we look at consumers' expectations. We try to understand exactly where those expectations stand between the short term and the medium term that is relevant for our monetary policy decisions. What we see at the moment is that short-term inflation expectations have risen in all three categories—more so for consumers, because that is typically the case. But when you look at the longer-term expectations, they are broadly anchored at our 2%. Whether you look at one-in-one, five-in-five, or one-in-five, you have the whole category of intervals to look at, and on all those accounts, inflation expectations are broadly anchored. So, you asked me about—I will call it artificial intelligence at large, but it's clearly the most recent development of artificial intelligence. Whether it's by Anthropic, or by OpenAI, or by, you know, other major developers and investors in artificial intelligence, or whether it's from other countries, we are seeing significantly new, faster, more intrusive developments. That is a fact. We also know that these developments are here to stay, and that they will continue to become more prominent in the coming months, and probably on an ongoing basis. So, that calls for, you know, two categories of responses. One addresses our own central bank. You know, are we at the ECB, and are the national central banks around in the Eurosystem, well-equipped to actually resist potential intrusion, potential hacking, or potential negative intrusion that would be using those tools? This is work that is underway, and this is one that we take very seriously because, obviously, we are a central part of the financial world. The second category, which is more under the remit of the SSM, has to do with how we help flag and call the attention of all the banks that we supervise, to make sure that they too—and I know that they do—pay particular attention in order to protect themselves, and make sure that possible intrusions that operate at a fast pace are not going to hurt them. So, I think everybody is looking at this very carefully. We do not all have access to the latest systems and tools of some of those developers that I have mentioned, but there are ways—not necessarily using the ultimate state-of-the-art and latest versions—to actually protect ourselves, do the necessary patching, and make sure that we are as secure as possible. All of that is taking place, so that's one category. I think there is another category that is, you know, worth discussing, which has to do with how governments and sovereigns actually respond to these risks, of which I think we are seeing maybe only the tip of the iceberg, and for which more significant risks should be anticipated. Possibly a joint framework or joint governance should be discussed at a higher level. But for the moment, at our limited level as a central bank, we do what we can in response to these developments that are here to stay. Thank you. Last question today goes to Arturo Lopo of the Spanish broadcaster RTVE. Arturo, please. Thank you for taking my question. Mrs. Lagarde, what would need to happen to make today's rate hike decision reverse? Under these current circumstances of massive uncertainty, how much time would the ECB or the Governing Council need to discuss or to consider reversing this rate hike? And as a second question: in this case, you have talked about how this is not an "insurance" rate hike, this is not a cycle, and this is not the beginning of a cycle. But at the same time, what are the other components that could play a bigger role in pushing up the floor of the interest rates in the eurozone? What is the future "good place to be" as you have been talking about? Under these circumstances, what is the new 2% for interest rates here in the eurozone? Thank you. If you look at our monetary policy statement, we say very specifically that with this 25 basis point increase in interest rates, we are well-positioned to navigate the uncertainty and the developments going forward. So, that stands very clearly in the MPS. As to the rest, we will be deciding meeting-by-meeting. We will be data-dependent, and we will have no preset, predetermined rate path going forward. And that's the way we will be operating. Thank you. Thank you very much, that closes our press conference. We will be back here on the 23rd of July, and until then, all the best and have a good afternoon.

How to navigate a crisis: lessons from a chief investment officer

World Economic Forum(1.1M)·33:49·366 views·2026-06-11
Yves BonzonGroup Chief Investment Officer, Julius Baer Bernadette AnderkoHost, Moving Markets
TL;DRSuccessfully navigating market crises requires distinguishing between unpredictable exogenous shocks and systemic endogenous failures, maintaining rigorous rational discipline over emotional behavioral biases, and retaining the portfolio flexibility ...
to pivot quickly when...

TL;DR

Successfully navigating market crises requires distinguishing between unpredictable exogenous shocks and systemic endogenous failures, maintaining rigorous rational discipline over emotional behavioral biases, and retaining the portfolio flexibility to pivot quickly when initial assumptions prove wrong.

The Anatomy of Market Shocks: Exogenous vs. Endogenous

  • Exogenous Shocks: External, unpredictable events (e.g., the COVID-19 pandemic or sudden geopolitical conflicts) that strike outside the economic system.
      • Secondary Consequences: While the initial shock is unpredictable, it can trigger highly impactful endogenous policy responses. For example, the massive, prolonged fiscal-monetary stimulus implemented during COVID-19 ultimately drove global inflation.
  • Endogenous Crises: Structural failures originating within the financial system itself, such as the 2008 Great Financial Crisis (GFC).
      • Predictability & Propagation: These crises are analytically easier to manage because the interconnections between balance sheets, propagation mechanisms, and the conditions required to contain the system-wide "wildfire" are better understood.

Case Studies in Crisis Management: 2008 vs. Geopolitical Conflicts

  • The 2008 Great Financial Crisis: Yves Bonzon highlights 2008 as the most "enjoyable" crisis of his career to navigate due to its endogenous nature.
      • Mitigating Drawdowns: Understanding the flaws in subprime mortgage securitization allowed Julius Baer to limit portfolio drawdowns during the panic phase and normalize risk rapidly at the market's tipping point in late March/early April 2009, capturing significant upside.
      • Internal Information Silos: A key systemic surprise was that major investment banks hoarded "pseudo-AAA" toxic assets on their own balance sheets. This occurred because manufacturing departments and treasury operations departments functioned in silos and failed to communicate.
  • The Middle East / Iran Conflict: Market reaction to recent geopolitical tensions has been mild, characterized by contained equity risk premia and muted volatility.
      • Structural Buffers: Unlike the 1973–1974 oil crisis, the global energy sector is currently buffered by high oil inventories, structural oversupply, and a lower global GDP oil intensity.
      • Systemic Resilience: The Western and Japanese private sectors (corporates and households) have significantly deleveraged since 2008. Consequently, the current system is far more resilient to external energy and geopolitical shocks.

Wealth Management Philosophy & Risk Dynamics

  • The Goal of Relative Wealth: In private wealth management, the ultimate mandate is to keep clients rich in relative terms.
      • MSCI World Benchmark: For ultra-high-net-worth individuals, lagging behind major indices like the MSCI World represents a real loss of purchasing power and relative financial standing compared to their peers.
  • The Value of Volatility: A reasonable degree of portfolio volatility is healthy and necessary to compound long-term returns.
      • Flexibility as Robustness: Portfolios must remain structurally flexible. In physics and finance, flexible structures are far more robust than completely rigid ones.
  • Agility Over Prediction: Success does not require a perfect crystal ball. Portfolio managers must possess the operational agility to add or reduce risk incrementally and reverse decisions quickly if they are proven wrong.

Overcoming Cognitive and Behavioral Biases

  • The Emotional Brain vs. Rational Brain: Drawing on the work of Nobel laureate Daniel Kahneman, Bonzon notes that human brains are hardwired for Stone Age survival, causing emotional fight-or-flight responses during market drawdowns. The primary role of a CIO is to enforce rational discipline when panic sets in.
  • The "Double-Decision" Trap: Emotional investors who panic-sell during market drawdowns often forget that market timing requires two perfect decisions: knowing when to exit, and knowing exactly when to re-enter. Mathematically, very few investors can execute both correctly.
  • The Anchoring Bias: Following a liquidity event (such as an IPO or M&A transaction), investors often become counterproductively obsessed with the specific cash figure crystallized, distorting future risk-taking decisions.
  • The Curse of Experience: Long-tenured investors must guard against falsely projecting past historical templates onto new situations. For example, treating today’s "Magnificent Seven" tech leaders as a simple rerun of the 1999–2000 dot-com bubble overlooks fundamental structural differences.

Notable Quotes

"When the weather is beautiful and the sea is quiet, everybody's a good sailor. It's in rough times that you can make a difference." — Yves Bonzon
"Most important is not to predict what happens next month. Most important is if you are wrong, not to stay wrong." — Yves Bonzon
"Something that is flexible is actually more robust physically than something that is completely rigid." — Yves Bonzon

Even a crisis you anticipate and you think it's a matter of when, not if, there are surprises along the way. And in a risky world, he has advice on how we should approach risk.
