But we have it with a lens of what are the big challenges facing the world and how can we overcome them. And every week we're talking about a different issue and trying to find better ways of doing things. Maybe for people who are listening to this on Radio Davos, Bernadette, you can tell us something about your podcast. Indeed, yes. So The View Beyond is the weekend edition of our daily Moving Markets show. And on that podcast, we're very much focused on what's been happening in financial markets in the previous 24 hours as well as touching on why. The View Beyond gives us the opportunity to dive deeper into a specific topic that's been on investors' minds during the week and allows us a little more breathing space to interrogate the subject with our expert. Great. Well, really happy to be doing this joint program. And on this joint podcast, we're looking at navigating crises. And to talk about that, Bernadette has found us a great guest. Indeed I have: Julius Baer's Group Chief Investment Officer, Yves Bonzon, who knows a lot about the difficult issue of investing throughout crises. Hello, Yves, and thank you for joining us. Hello Bernadette, hi Robin. Thanks for having me. So Yves, as someone who's been a CIO, I think for more than 30 years now, and with even more experience managing investment decisions on behalf of private clients, you've had the unenviable task of having to navigate multiple crises. We've had the Asian crisis of 1997, the global financial crisis in 2008, of course COVID in 2020, and now unfortunately the US-Iran war. Perhaps I could start by asking if you've got some sort of rulebook as to how you react when sudden shocks hit financial markets? I mean, are there specific steps that you follow, things that you particularly analyze before you make those first actions? The first point I'd like to make is that every crisis is different. And most investors see crisis as a danger. Unfortunately, or fortunately, every crisis is different in terms of the conditions that lead to that crisis, the catalyst and the driver, the forces at work in the system during the crisis, as well as, critically, policymakers' response—whether adequate in size and nature or not—to the crisis. And I know that you often make reference to exogenous and endogenous shocks because I've read it enough in your weeklies and monthlies. So shocks that come either from outside or within the economic system itself. But does that make a difference then to how you shape your reaction? It does at two levels. In theory, first of all, you cannot predict external shocks. These are exogenous to the system. So that creates an opportunity, especially when you manage private client assets, private wealth. Our clients typically have a very solid balance sheet with some untapped risk-taking capacity. The problem is some of these exogenous shocks can have endogenous consequences. A good example is COVID in the recent past. You know, suddenly there is this virus, the pandemic starts. This is not predictable. This hits economies. Of course, the first policymaker's decision is the shutdown to protect the healthcare system from being flooded with severely affected patients. That's a policy decision. Then there is a second critical policy decision, which is that massive combined fiscal-monetary stimulus. And the way and how that stimulus is implemented, in particular the size of the stimulus and the duration of the support, of course, matters. In the case of COVID, probably the size was too big, the duration was too long, and this led to a stronger inflation impact than expected in the economy. So an exogenous shock like this can lead to endogenous consequences. And there, it's an important distinction. Finally, endogenous crises, and you mentioned 2008, the Great Financial Crisis. This is totally endogenous, you know. We have a much better understanding of endogenous mechanisms, the impact on the system, and how this will impact the balance sheets in which we invest, because as investors we fund balance sheets and all balance sheets are interconnected with each other, right? So we understand better how a crisis, sort of a wildfire like 2008, propagates, and we also understand what type of conditions must be fulfilled for the fire to be contained. We're in a crisis right now, and I think some of what you've just been saying can be seen in the way governments or individuals and investors are looking out on the outlook of the Iran war. And we can see it every day or every minute in the oil price going up and down, and a lot of that seems to be people wondering when the thing will end. So my question to you, Yves, is how do you distinguish between a temporary shock that's going to change things like the oil price in this case and then lots of other things, or something that's now going to be fixed in for the medium term, and kind of what difference does that make to the decisions you're making? The current crisis is an interesting example because you can draw parallels with what happened in 1973–1974 with the oil shock at the time. So you have this exogenous shock, the start of the war in Iran. And this time around, the first observation we made is the reaction of capital markets. The rise in equity risk premia and volatility is rather mild, muted, contained. So even if it's a temporary shock, we didn't assess the shock at the time as a great opportunity to add risk, you know, in a favorable timing, simply because the reaction was not really very significant. At a second stage, later during the month of March, the conflict started to target infrastructure, energy infrastructure. And there, our degree of concern increased slightly because closing the Strait of Hormuz is a decision you can reverse reasonably quickly. Damaged infrastructure takes time to repair. But of course the circumstances, such as the petrol intensity in GDP today compared to the 1970s, the circumstances are also different. And pretty quickly markets have been discounting a robust energy infrastructure and system globally. Many countries produce oil. By the way, the starting point was very, very ample inventories of oil and refined products and a market in structural oversupply. So that's a favorable starting point. And very quickly the market started to discount a rebalancing of energy. Admittedly, an oil shock of some 30–40 dollars can be absorbed. And that's where the conditions in the system, and I refer again to balance sheets... One of the absolutely critical things to have in mind today is that in the West, primarily the US, Europe, but as well Japan, in developed economies, private sector balance sheets—and this applies equally to corporate balance sheets and household individuals, private individuals' balance sheets—are strong because since the post-financial crisis time, the private sector in the developed world has deleveraged. So this shock nowadays hits a system which is deleveraged and the impact therefore is much less severe because the system is much more resilient to those shocks. Long story short, you have to take all of those elements into the equation to assess whether it's an opportunity to increase risk and take advantage of the risk aversion spike, or if you should hedge. And very critically, you manage that by gradually adding or reducing risk in such a situation, with the ability to quickly reverse your decision. Most important is not to predict what happens next month. Most important is, if you are wrong, not to stay wrong. I mentioned other crises that you've had to navigate in your career, Yves. Is there one that stands out as being particularly memorable, and if yes, perhaps you'd explain why? By far the most interesting from a personal and career perspective was 2008. Because 2008 being endogenous in nature, here is a crisis that if you have the knowledge, if you have the right counterparties to support and help you understand the mechanism and forces at work in the system, you can really take advantage. By taking advantage, I mean you can mitigate the drawdown of your portfolios during the phase where the crisis is intensifying. And when we are reaching the climax and we are on the tipping point of the crisis—obviously, it was late March, early April '09—you can normalize very quickly your risk load, which leads to very good performance and a swift recovery of portfolio values. So you've actually enhanced returns by taking advantage of the crisis. And of course, this involves being temporarily in a low double-digit drawdown in most portfolios. But again, you construct future performance by taking advantage of overly stressed valuations during such market episodes. And by any measure as far as I'm concerned, in those crises I have experienced—the first one was the crash of '87—2008 is really a crisis I really enjoyed a lot steering the ship as a CIO. The tariffs thing was much more difficult because you're dealing with a volatile administration, and it's not clear what they are up to. It's exogenous in nature, back to the nuance between exogenous and endogenous, and much more difficult to navigate. I'm interested in this idea of an enjoyable crisis from your point of view and one that's less so. It seems to be that if it's more predictable once the shock has happened, like in 2008, then you can use your powers of analysis and your team of analysts to work out where the best course of action lies. But if you're facing lots of uncertainty, as with Washington's tariff regime which has changed so, so often, that's difficult because you're not relying on kind of hard facts. Is that basically what you're saying? The first observation I'd like to make, and drawing a parallel between CIOs and statesmen—with all the usual reserves, you know—is there is no such thing as great political leaders without a crisis. And everybody would have long forgotten Winston Churchill if it was only for his role in the First World War. It's really the Second World War that made him one of the all-time great and famous political leaders of modern times. So the crisis is an opportunity for somebody who loves what he does and is good at it to make a difference. When the weather is beautiful and the sea is quiet, everybody is a good sailor, right? It's in rough times that you can make a difference. Hence my qualification of enjoying the crisis. Yes, because that's when you can make a difference, right? It reminds me of, you know, I'm a news reporter at heart and it's always... You love crisis too, do you? You know, I'm ambivalent, but I know exactly what you're saying. You know, above and beyond that, Robin, is the issue of how our human brain works. You know, and that's the teaching of Nobel Prize winner Daniel Kahneman. Our brain was built, you know, framed and programmed for survival in the wild times of the Stone Age. And we have developed two brains: the emotional brain and the rational brain. And of course, emotions take over during crises, and it's really an opportunity if you can stay rational to make a difference. I'm going to hand back to Bernadette in a moment. There's one more I was going to ask you. You've already mentioned you've got a crisis on your hands, this kind of two things: it's a threat, so it's a threat to the capital you're managing, but it also can be an opportunity. There will be places where if you invest in certain areas you're going to get better returns because maybe other people aren't investing in those areas, for example, or you've seen an opportunity that others haven't seen. Do those things go hand in hand for you, or do you first think, "Right, we've got to make sure this capital's safe," and then a few days later we're thinking, "Okay, let's look at the opportunities"? I mean, how do you kind of weigh the two things? So one needs to understand what his or her mandate is. If you're a hedge fund manager and you have a formula for compensation purposes, earning a cut on your profit and loss statement by year-end, your time horizon is 12 months. Obviously, you're a trader; you have really an absolute return mindset and absolute return investment objective. We are wealth managers. Our mission is to make sure our clients stay rich. They got rich primarily in two fields: real estate or, more often, entrepreneurship, building companies. These are the two main sources of wealth creation in the economy. And our value proposition, our duty to them, is to help them stay rich in relative terms. I always tell our clients: in life and markets, everything is relative. Everything is relative. And especially, for instance, if you help ultra-high-net-worth individuals manage their money, I always tell them, look, your benchmark is MSCI World, you know. Your peers are large shareholders in the companies that populate those famous and leading world equity indices. And that's your cost of capital. Meaning, if you lag MSCI World by a significant margin on a relative basis, you're getting poorer relative to your peers. And by the time you want to buy that very nice property in Tuscany, there will be other bidders with more firepower than you. The point I'm getting at, it's really a relative question philosophically. And that raises the topic of drawdown and all the investment biases, behavioral biases associated with them. One of which is the typical anchoring, you know. Here comes a liquidity event, somebody crystallizes a large amount of money in that transaction—an initial public offering or a corporate M&A transaction. And suddenly the number that has been crystallized becomes a sort of obsession. This is extremely counterproductive. Of course you want to mitigate drawdown, because every time markets recover and you start to compound returns again, you want to compound off the highest possible base for the future. But a reasonable degree of volatility is not only normal but actually warranted in a portfolio. Something that is flexible is actually more robust physically than something that is completely rigid, by the way. When is a crisis a crisis? Sometimes things come totally out of the blue, but I guess that's fairly rare. Normally there's some kind of build-up. Even in COVID, we were getting reports of a virus happening somewhere in a town in China, and it took a few weeks perhaps for the penny to drop with investors. Or did it? Another example would be in a war situation. You see a build-up of military equipment on the border with a country like Ukraine, and Russia is saying, "Oh, they're not going to invade." But then suddenly they do, and that becomes a crisis. I wonder, when do you decide, "Oh, this issue's a crisis and it's really going to change the way we are acting in various ways"? So again, a very different approach depending on whether the build-up into the crisis is of endogenous or exogenous nature. You mentioned COVID as an example. There have been so many other virus cases that have gone essentially nowhere that it's extremely difficult to say, "Oh, this time the virus is serious enough we need to pay attention." Suddenly it woke up one morning and said, "Whoa, this time it's for real, it's the remake of the Spanish flu movie." Do you remember in that case what was that moment? Why suddenly did the market... It was, I remember, sometime in February 2020. And you can tell because it's the first drawdown leg in leading equity indices, right, when the market woke up to that and realized it's very serious. But of course, the way and how the market responds and the depth of the drawdown was at the time directly a function of the decision very quickly thereafter by governments to go into shutdown mode. That was not expected either. By the way, during the Spanish flu nobody went into shutdown mode, you know. It was a different time, and with different communication means, I guess, and a much lesser ability, you know. Without the digital economy and if the internet had not yet been invented, I think the appetite for shutdown would have been far more limited among governments. Back to a good example of an endogenous crisis: the financial crisis of 2008, the banking crisis. This one we saw coming three to four years in advance. And you know, the system continued to build. One of the reasons we saw it coming is because we were invested with a few managers who had a very good understanding of the pitfalls and flaws in the securitization of subprime mortgages. But even in this very, very historic example, even though we understood the flaws in securitization of subprime, we did not expect to find them on banks' balance sheets. Let me explain. So we knew these assets were not AAA-rated bonds, you know, or bond equivalents. And we knew some naive money was buying them in belief they were AAA when they were not. But we thought that those who manufacture those instruments, the investment banks, knew that these were not real AAA, that these were fake AAAs, right? Actually, they didn't know—except the department managing the treasury of those banks is in a different area of the city from the department manufacturing them, and they were not talking to each other. So as some colleagues were busy manufacturing those pseudo-AAA papers, treasury operations were happily hoarding them on the balance sheet, right? So you see, even a crisis you anticipate and you think it's a matter of when, not if, there are surprises along the way. It does not necessarily play out as per a pre-designed script. And of course, the fact that the financial infrastructure was impaired made the crisis much worse than it would have been otherwise, you know. Otherwise, it would have been a shock on housing, a negative wealth impact on American household balance sheets, which of course is negative for consumption, and let's say we would have had an average recession. The fact that the financial system was impaired led to a deep recession. That's one of the highlights of your career of dealing with crises, Yves. You also mentioned, you know, your career stretches back to the late 80s. The world has changed a lot over that time. You mentioned the COVID situation; countries wouldn't have been able to lock down if we weren't all able to do at least some of our business and our communications online. Things have sped up when it comes to trading and communications, and we're now in the era of AI. I just wonder if you notice over the years, since the 80s and through to today, how crises develop differently, and how your approach to crises, would you say, is different today than it would have been in 1990? I don't think my approach would have been different. I think on a personal level there is a rare group among investment professionals that has inherited a very strong intuitive intelligence ability. That's not the topic of this podcast, so let's not get into this. I would tend to believe Stanley is right: you have it or you don't have it. So it is very important to keep that intuitive intelligence skill, but making sure that you are not actually overly biased and influenced by your personal, professional, or investor's life experience, falling into the trap of believing you are identifying patterns you've already seen but which actually are different. And things that you would highlight perhaps to be wary of. I mean, when we look at behavioral finance, and I know I'm guilty of this myself, sometimes we make emotional decisions when we're making investment decisions. Absolutely. Those decisions can prove immensely expensive. And by the way, they're not specific to crises. They happen every day. When is the best time to invest? This morning. And the next best? Next morning. The point I'm getting at is individuals have a tendency, not all of them but most of them, to be overly risk-averse to drawdowns, to have a poor understanding of the statistical distribution they are confronting in the game of investing they are playing, and as a result, leave a lot of money on the table. Snowflake Summit 2026 Platform Keynote

Snowflake Inc.(63.7K)·97:31·645 views·2026-06-11
Benoit DagevilleCo-Founder, Snowflake Christian KleinermanEVP of Product, Snowflake
TL;DR1.
TL;DR The Snowflake Summit 2026 Platform Keynote unveiled the transition of Snowflake from a unified data repository to a fully integrated agentic enterprise engine, spearheaded by the rebranding of its AI capabilities into Snowflake CoCo (developer/coding) and Snowflake...

1. TL;DR

The Snowflake Summit 2026 Platform Keynote unveiled the transition of Snowflake from a unified data repository to a fully integrated agentic enterprise engine, spearheaded by the rebranding of its AI capabilities into Snowflake CoCo (developer/coding) and Snowflake CoWork (business collaboration). Key technical breakthroughs include the introduction of Snowflake Datastream, a 40x faster Interactive Compiler, and deep-security guardrails designed to secure and optimize AI agents on a single trusted data foundation.

2. ## The AI Control Plane: Introducing CoCo and CoWork

Snowflake has consolidated and simplified its AI surface area into two distinct, highly integrated agentic workspaces to streamline developer and business user workflows.

  • Snowflake CoCo (formerly Cortex Code): Rebranded to represent Snowflake’s AI-powered coding, development, and system operations assistant.
      • New Capabilities: Features Cloud Agents (near General Availability), local development sandboxes for the CLI, and a "skill catalog" to share and discover plugins.
      • CoCo for Desktop: A new, locally running desktop application designed to build applications, create skills, and diagnose complex data pipelines in real time.
      • AI-Powered Migrations (AIM): CoCo now drives automated translation of legacy Spark (RDD) and Teradata SQL/BTEQ code directly into optimized Snowflake environments.
  • Snowflake CoWork (formerly Snowflake Intelligence): Rebranded as the universal, user-centric interface for business leaders and front-line workers.
      • Personal Work Engine: Eliminates manual agent selection by utilizing multi-agent orchestration, which automatically routes requests to the correct specialized agent.
      • User Memory: Enables CoWork to learn individual user preferences and patterns over time to contextualize future analytical responses.
      • Cortex Sense: A new runtime capability that boosts out-of-the-box coding agent accuracy from 24% to 83% by analyzing user activity and metadata already stored inside Snowflake.
      • Natoma Acquisition: Integrates native connectivity between CoWork/CoCo and over 100 enterprise business systems.

3. ## Performance Breakthroughs & Streaming Infrastructure

Snowflake introduced massive architectural upgrades to its core data engine, focusing on sub-second data ingestion, drastically reduced compile times, and near-instant query responsiveness.

  • Snowflake Datastream: A newly announced, fully managed streaming service built directly into Snowflake (entering private preview).
      • Architecture: Features separated storage and compute, delivering zero-copy, sub-second latency streaming that is fully Kafka wire-compatible.
  • Interactive Compiler: A brand-new query compiler designed for high-concurrency, low-latency workloads. Early benchmarks show a 40x faster compile time, leading to a 3x to 4x overall acceleration of workloads.
  • Adaptive Compute: Officially moving to General Availability (GA). The next-generation compute paradigm delivers up to 2x faster performance compared to original Generation 1 warehouses.
  • Unistore & Snowflake Postgres:
      • 8x Latency Improvement: An engine optimization for Unistore and hybrid tables has improved latency and throughput by 8x (public preview).
      • Postgres Data Mirroring: A simple, single-switch replication capability (public preview) that handles change data capture (CDC) and low-latency synchronization into Snowflake.

4. ## Trust, Intent-Driven Governance, and Agent Security

With the rise of autonomous AI agents, Snowflake has reinforced its Horizon governance suite with strict compliance boundaries and advanced security tooling.

  • Intent-Driven Governance: Utilizing CoCo, administrators can declare natural-language goals (e.g., *"protect all PII data in my database"*), and Snowflake automatically handles classification and policy enforcement.
  • Agent Identity Control: Snowflake now registers a distinct "agentic context" function, allowing administrators to restrict or mask sensitive data views specifically when accessed by AI agents.
  • Data Movement Policies: Enables teams to block specific tagged datasets from being downloaded via the UI or copied to external stages.
  • Multi-Party Approvals: Built for cyber resiliency and anti-ransomware protection, this private preview feature requires mandatory second-person verification for critical admin operations (such as disabling MFA).
  • Horizon Context: Automatically compiles semantic metadata, BI signals, and transformation logic to supply AI agents with complete enterprise context.

5. ## Interoperability, Open Sharing, and Zero-Copy Partnerships

Snowflake continues to champion open standards and zero-copy integrations, eliminating the need to move or duplicate data across external platforms.

  • Apache Iceberg V3 & V4: Snowflake reaffirmed its commitment to the open format by supporting the Apache Polaris REST catalog within Horizon, allowing bidirectional reads/writes across external catalogs and engines.
  • Open Sharing: A public preview feature leveraging Iceberg REST catalog APIs to allow Snowflake users to seamlessly share secure data assets with non-Snowflake consumers.
  • Multi-Party Collaboration: Clean room technology is now Generally Available, enabling multiple organizations to securely analyze co-mingled datasets under pre-defined operational roles.
  • Zero-Copy Ecosystem Expansion: SAP zero-copy integration is now Generally Available, alongside newly announced partnerships with Workday, IBM Watsonx (for DB2/mainframe data), and AVEVA Connect.

6. ## Enterprise Case Studies: AI in Production

Executive guests highlighted how they have successfully shifted AI from trial phases to live, high-impact production.

  • Thomson Reuters (Caitlin Halferty, VP): Deployed *Co-Counsel*, its flagship AI capability built on Snowflake's trusted data foundation, supporting over 1 million legal, tax, and audit professionals globally.
  • Under Armour (Patrick Duroseau, Chief Data & AI Officer): Developed the conversational AI assistant *Ada* to empower internal teams to query cross-functional data, moving beyond traditional static reporting.
  • Samsung (Jung Suh): Integrated the *Shopper's Insight Action Agent (SIA)* on Snowflake CoWork to drive "AX" (AI-Based Process Transformation). Over 1,000 global executives and marketers use the tool to instantly synthesize Amazon and global sales performance, bypassing weeks of manual reporting.

7. ## Notable Quotes

"Building an isolated AI stack repeats the exact same mistakes we talked about earlier: basically recreating silos, fracturing your governance, and driving up cost and complexity." — Benoit Dageville, Co-Founder and Chief Architect, Snowflake
"We want you to rethink what is possible. The art of the possible is changing... Snowflake will help you empower everyone in your organization, everyone, to leverage AI to be more productive." — Christian Kleinerman, EVP of Product, Snowflake
"That shift from the data team as a bottleneck to every leader as their own analyst operating in an AX [AI-based process transformation] model, that’s the transformation we came to Snowflake for." — Jung Suh, Samsung
"Governance for us has enabled us to de-risk and accelerate our AI transformation both internally and externally... we build to a fiduciary-grade standard." — Caitlin Halferty, Thomson Reuters
What happens when ideas meet intelligence? Progress is accelerated. Creativity is amplified. Inspiration becomes impact. Please welcome Snowflake co-founder and chief architect, Benoit Dageville. Good morning! Wow! Good morning! What you just saw wasn't magic. It was Cortex Code collaborating with R Tyler, our live coding DJ. A few years ago—yeah, a few years ago—that would have felt like science fiction. Today, that magic is reality. And it's exactly what we are here to talk about: turning your ideas to production and doing that fast. As Sridhar explained yesterday, the agentic enterprise is the ultimate path to get there. I now want to dive into the architectural foundation that makes this entire vision possible. Back in 2012, my co-founder Thierry and I knew that working with data was fundamentally broken. And it really came down to two reasons. First, data was siloed. Structured data was trapped in traditional data warehouses, while semi-structured data was relegated to Hadoop systems. To make matters worse, neither could scale beyond a single cluster. The moment you ran out of capacity, you had to spin up a separate system, creating yet another silo. Second, because data was scattered, maintaining consistent security and governance was a near-impossible task. Plus, these legacy systems were inherently complex, buried in endless performance knobs that required an army of specialists to tune. With Snowflake, we set out to fix this by creating one fully governed platform built on three foundational principles. First, all data. We engineered a system that could seamlessly query structured and semi-structured data together, effectively unifying the data warehouse and big data systems with unprecedented performance at a multi-petabyte scale. Next, all compute. By pioneering a revolutionary architecture that decouples compute from storage, we completely eliminated workload interference and redefined the economics. And the math is simple: basically, scaling your compute by, say, 10x delivers near-linear performance gains without increasing the cost. Finally, all users. We deliver a zero-maintenance, fully managed service that simply works for everyone. In 2016, we published our architecture at SIGMOD, the premier conference for data management. This year, that paper received the Test of Time Award. And it's really a validation that our founding principles continue to shape today's modern cloud data systems. If you think about it, this paper was a milestone, but it was really only the beginning of our journey. For over a decade, we have remained relentless in pursuing this original goal: basically, breaking down silos and eliminating every underlying reason for them to exist. It started by breaking down geographic boundaries. We went global, making Snowflake cross-cloud and cross-region so that your data is never trapped by a cloud provider or a physical location. From there, we enabled frictionless data sharing. We built a global data network and marketplace where collaborating with external data is just as seamless as working with your own data. Then, we embraced Apache Iceberg, which ensures full interoperability and unified governance for your entire data estate, even when data lives in open formats outside of Snowflake. Finally, we expanded to unstructured data. Thanks to AI, documents, audio, images, and even videos are now native citizens of the Snowflake platform, living seamlessly alongside structured and semi-structured data. But data is only half of the story. Even if your data is perfectly unified, silos will still creep in if your platform can't support a broad spectrum of workloads. So, we expanded our compute capabilities in several ways. First, we broke language boundaries. We introduced Snowpark to complement SQL, giving Python, Java, and Scala developers full access to the platform with the exact same performance and governance. Second, we unified transactional and analytical workloads. We built Unistore and Snowflake Postgres right into the platform so you no longer need separate operational databases. And third, we fully host applications. With Snowpark Container Services, you can now run any containerized application or even complex AI workloads directly inside the platform, right next to your data. Bringing all data, all workloads, and all users together under one unified governance model was a massive expansion. Through all of it, we doubled down on making it as easy as possible to use. But we didn't stop there. As I mentioned, we have now entered the era of the agentic enterprise. To thrive in this new era, you really need two things. First, the foundation: the world's best AI agents must be powered by the world's best data platform. Second, you need a unified architecture. Both AI and data must live on a single, integrated platform. Building an isolated AI stack repeats the exact same mistakes we talked about earlier: basically recreating silos, fracturing your governance, and driving up cost and complexity. What's more, having a platform unifying AI and data unlocks the ultimate flywheel. Your data makes your AI platform incredibly smart, which, in turn, makes your entire data platform faster, simpler, and infinitely productive. By uniting AI and data, the Snowflake AI Data Cloud delivers the ideal foundation to power your agentic enterprise. What is Snowflake Datastream? It is a fully managed streaming service built directly into Snowflake. In true Snowflake fashion, it has a separation of storage and compute. It does zero-copy streaming, which lets you stream data to and from Snowflake with sub-second latency. Most importantly, it is Kafka wire-compatible, so all your clients and applications can stream into Datastream directly. Of course, we have unified streaming and analytics, where you can instantiate a topic directly into a Snowflake table. This will be in private preview shortly. AI has also changed how we think about migrations. We've now unified all of our migration efforts under the term AIM: AI-powered migrations. In the era of agents, we want to make sure all of you can secure your agents, that you have multiple levels of protection, and, of course, built-in security so you can sleep well at night. We introduced Horizon AI Guardrails earlier in the year, which provides protections in both CoCo and CoWork to prevent high-risk threats, such as jailbreaking or prompt injection attacks. This is built-in, detecting zero-day attacks, and it gives you some policies and some control because we want to make sure that you are all protected. Today, we're also introducing the concept of agent identity, so you can tell when some piece of code or some activity in Snowflake is happening under an agent context. We give you a context function, which is what you see here. For example, in a masking policy or in a row policy, you can say, "If it is an agentic context, maybe you give it less visibility, or maybe you give it more"—whatever you want to do. We want to give you that control over what agents are doing. We're also introducing data movement policies, so you can say, "This data that has this tag shall not move to a stage, internal or external, or shall not be downloaded in the Snowsight UI." You are in control. Policies are there to help you be in control of what you want AI and agents to do. We also have a detection package in the Trust Center, in public preview now, that helps you monitor unusual data transfers, because we all want to know if data is moving outside of our secure perimeter. A few months ago, we introduced the concept of backups as a way to create an immutable, point-in-time snapshot of an object, a schema, or a database. We also introduced the concept of a retention lock, which makes it impossible to delete—not even an account admin can delete it. Why is that useful? It is part of a cyber-resiliency strategy and part of an anti-ransomware posture. Today, we're also introducing multi-party approvals, where you can require a mandatory second-person verification for certain highly sensitive operations—you decide which ones. So, even a rogue insider admin or an agent trying to change something cannot execute highly sensitive operations, like disabling MFA for everyone; it cannot be done without two administrators in the system agreeing on it. This is in private preview now. We're also adding a number of AI security checks to the Trust Center to enable all of you to check for what we consider best practices in both the configuration of agents and the configuration of AI. But you may have heard from us and from others that what really makes AI work, especially in the enterprise context, is exactly that: understanding your data, understanding context. That is why today we're introducing Horizon Context. Intelligence alone is not enough, and oftentimes what you really miss is that context. Horizon Context is part of the Horizon Catalog—it's not a different technology—and it helps collect signals, enrich those signals, and make them available to CoCo, CoWork, or Cortex agents so you can get more context and more semantic information. A lot of what we've been doing with semantic views continues to move forward and is part of Horizon Context. We keep innovating—the team has done dozens of launches to improve the expressive power of semantic views. We're also introducing a number of metadata connectors that become part of Horizon Context, where you can get context from BI tools, data transformation tools, or other databases. All in all, Horizon is the place where we innovate to help you govern your data and govern what AI does.
